<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ABC_77_W66_jeh.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/ABC_77_W66_jeh.JPG/220px-ABC_77_W66_jeh.JPG" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/ABC_77_W66_jeh.JPG/330px-ABC_77_W66_jeh.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/ABC_77_W66_jeh.JPG/440px-ABC_77_W66_jeh.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2845" data-file-height="2224" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ABC_77_W66_jeh.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
ABC's corporate headquarters are located at 77 West 66th Street, on the <a href="/wiki/Upper_West_Side" title="Upper West Side">Upper West Side</a> of <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <b>American Broadcasting Company</b> (<b>ABC</b>) is an American <a href="/wiki/Commercial_broadcasting" title="Commercial broadcasting">commercial broadcast</a> <a href="/wiki/Television_network" title="Television network">television network</a> that is owned by the <a href="/wiki/Disney%E2%80%93ABC_Television_Group" title="Disney–ABC Television Group">Disney–ABC Television Group</a>, a subsidiary of the <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Company#Disney_Media_Networks" title="The Walt Disney Company">Disney Media Networks</a> division of <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Company" title="The Walt Disney Company">The Walt Disney Company</a>. The network is headquartered on <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Avenue_(Manhattan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbus Avenue (Manhattan)">Columbus Avenue</a> and <a href="/wiki/66th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="66th Street (Manhattan)">West 66th Street</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>. There are additional major offices and production facilities elsewhere in New York City, as well as in <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burbank,_California" title="Burbank, California">Burbank, California</a>.</p>
<p>Since 2007, when <a href="/wiki/Cumulus_Media_Networks" title="Cumulus Media Networks">ABC Radio</a> (also known as Cumulus Media Networks) was sold to <a href="/wiki/Citadel_Broadcasting" title="Citadel Broadcasting">Citadel Broadcasting</a>, ABC has reduced its broadcasting operations almost exclusively to television. The fifth-oldest major broadcasting network in the world and the youngest of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Three_television_networks" title="Big Three television networks">Big Three television networks</a>, ABC is often nicknamed as "<b>The Alphabet Network</b>", as its <a href="/wiki/Acronym#Nomenclature" title="Acronym">initialism</a> also represents the first three letters of the <a href="/wiki/English_alphabet" title="English alphabet">English alphabet</a>, in order.</p>
<p>ABC originally launched on October 12, 1943, as a <a href="/wiki/Radio_network" title="Radio network">radio network</a>, separated from and serving as the successor to the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Network" title="Blue Network">NBC Blue Network</a>, which had been purchased by <a href="/wiki/Edward_John_Noble" title="Edward John Noble">Edward J. Noble</a>. It extended its operations to television in 1948, following in the footsteps of established broadcast networks <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a> and <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">NBC</a>. In the mid-1950s, ABC merged with <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting-Paramount_Theatres" title="American Broadcasting-Paramount Theatres">United Paramount Theatres</a>, a chain of movie theaters that formerly operated as a subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Pictures" title="Paramount Pictures">Paramount Pictures</a>. <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Goldenson" title="Leonard Goldenson">Leonard Goldenson</a>, who had been the head of UPT, made the new television network profitable by helping develop and greenlight many successful series. In the 1980s, after purchasing an 80% interest in cable sports channel <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">ESPN</a>, the network's corporate parent, American Broadcasting Companies, Inc., merged with <a href="/wiki/Capital_Cities_Communications" title="Capital Cities Communications">Capital Cities Communications</a>, owner of several print publications, and television and radio stations. In 1996, most of Capital Cities/ABC's assets were purchased by The Walt Disney Company.</p>
<p>The television network has eight <a href="/wiki/Owned-and-operated_station" title="Owned-and-operated station">owned-and-operated</a> and over 232 <a href="/wiki/Network_affiliate" title="Network affiliate">affiliated</a> television stations throughout the United States and its territories. Most <a href="/wiki/Canadians" title="Canadians">Canadians</a> have access to at least one U.S.-based ABC affiliate, either over-the-air (in areas located within proximity to the <a href="/wiki/Canada%E2%80%93United_States_border" title="Canada–United States border">Canada–United States border</a>) or through a <a href="/wiki/Cable_television" title="Cable television">cable</a>, <a href="/wiki/Direct_broadcast_satellite" class="mw-redirect" title="Direct broadcast satellite">satellite</a> or <a href="/wiki/IPTV" title="IPTV">IPTV</a> provider, although most ABC programs are subject to <a href="/wiki/Simultaneous_substitution" title="Simultaneous substitution">simultaneous substitution</a> regulations imposed by the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Radio-television_and_Telecommunications_Commission" title="Canadian Radio-television and Telecommunications Commission">Canadian Radio-television and Telecommunications Commission</a> that allow pay television providers to replace an American station's signal with the feed of a Canadian broadcaster to protect domestic programming rights and advertising revenue. <a href="/wiki/ABC_News" title="ABC News">ABC News</a> provides news and features content for select radio stations owned by <a href="/wiki/Citadel_Broadcasting" title="Citadel Broadcasting">Citadel Broadcasting</a>, which purchased the ABC Radio properties in 2007.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Broadcasting_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blue_Network_.281927.E2.80.931945.29">Blue Network (1927–1945)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Broadcasting_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Blue Network (1927–1945)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Blue_Network" title="Blue Network">Blue Network</a></div>
<p>In the 1930s, radio in the United States was dominated by three companies: the <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">Columbia Broadcasting System</a> (CBS), the <a href="/wiki/Mutual_Broadcasting_System" title="Mutual Broadcasting System">Mutual Broadcasting System</a> and the <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">National Broadcasting Company</a> (NBC). The last was owned by electronics manufacturer <a href="/wiki/RCA" title="RCA">Radio Corporation of America</a> (RCA), which owned two radio networks that each ran different varieties of programming, <a href="/wiki/Blue_Network" title="Blue Network">NBC Blue</a> and <a href="/wiki/NBC_Red_Network" title="NBC Red Network">NBC Red</a>. The NBC Blue Network was created in 1927 for the primary purpose of testing new programs on markets of lesser importance than those served by NBC Red, which served the major cities,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199396_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199396-4">[4]</a></sup> and to test drama series.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESterling2004291_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESterling2004291-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1934, Mutual filed a complaint with the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission" title="Federal Communications Commission">Federal Communications Commission</a> (FCC) regarding its difficulties in establishing new stations, in a radio market that was already being saturated by NBC and CBS.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESterling2004291_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESterling2004291-5">[5]</a></sup> In 1938, the FCC began a series of investigations into the practices of radio networks<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESterling2004291_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESterling2004291-5">[5]</a></sup> and published its report on the broadcasting of network radio programs in 1940. The report recommended that RCA give up control of either NBC Red or NBC Blue.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199396_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199396-4">[4]</a></sup> At that time, the NBC Red Network was the principal radio network in the United States and, according to the FCC, RCA was using NBC Blue to eliminate any hint of competition. Having no power over the networks themselves, the FCC established a regulation forbidding licenses to be issued for radio stations if they were affiliated with a network which already owned multiple networks<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199396_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199396-4">[4]</a></sup> that provided content of public interest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESterling2004291_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESterling2004291-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:114px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_J._Noble.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d4/Edward_J._Noble.jpg" width="112" height="158" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="112" data-file-height="158" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_J._Noble.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Edward_John_Noble" title="Edward John Noble">Edward Noble</a>, founder of ABC</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Once Mutual's appeals against the FCC were rejected, RCA decided to sell NBC Blue in 1941, and gave the mandate to do so to Mark Woods.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECox200991_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECox200991-6">[6]</a></sup> RCA converted the NBC Blue Network into an independent subsidiary, formally divorcing the operations of NBC Red and NBC Blue on January 8, 1942,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESterling2004291_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESterling2004291-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> with the Blue Network being referred to on-air as either "Blue" or "Blue Network".<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The newly separated NBC Red and NBC Blue divided their respective corporate assets. Between 1942 and 1943, Woods offered to sell the entire NBC Blue Network,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199397_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199397-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnouw1968190_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnouw1968190-10">[10]</a></sup> a package that included leases on landlines, three pending television licenses (WJZ-TV in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, <a href="/wiki/KGO-TV" title="KGO-TV">KGO-TV</a> in <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a> and <a href="/wiki/WLS-TV" title="WLS-TV">WENR-TV</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>), 60 affiliates, four operations facilities (in New York City, Chicago, Los Angeles and Washington D.C.), contracts with actors, and the brand associated with the Blue Network. Investment firm <a href="/wiki/Dillon,_Read_%26_Co." title="Dillon, Read &amp; Co.">Dillon, Read &amp; Co.</a> (which was later acquired by the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Bank_Corporation" title="Swiss Bank Corporation">Swiss Bank Corporation</a> in 1997) offered $7.5 million to purchase the network, but the offer was rejected by Woods and RCA president <a href="/wiki/David_Sarnoff" title="David Sarnoff">David Sarnoff</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199397_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199397-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Edward_John_Noble" title="Edward John Noble">Edward John Noble</a>, the owner of <a href="/wiki/Life_Savers" title="Life Savers">Life Savers</a> candy, drugstore chain <a href="/wiki/Rexall" title="Rexall">Rexall</a> and New York City radio station <a href="/wiki/WMCA_(AM)" title="WMCA (AM)">WMCA</a>, purchased the network for $8 million.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199396_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199396-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESterling2004291_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESterling2004291-5">[5]</a></sup> Due to FCC ownership rules, the transaction, which was to include the purchase of three RCA stations by Noble, would require him to resell his station with the FCC's approval.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199397_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199397-9">[9]</a></sup> The Commission authorized the transaction on October 12, 1943.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECox200991_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECox200991-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GoldenAgeNBC_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoldenAgeNBC-11">[11]</a></sup> Soon afterward, the Blue Network was purchased by the new company Noble founded, the American Broadcasting System.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESterling2004291_5-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESterling2004291-5">[5]</a></sup> Noble subsequently acquired the rights to the "American Broadcasting Company" name from <a href="/wiki/Storer_Communications" title="Storer Communications">George B. Storer</a> in 1944; its parent company adopted the corporate name American Broadcasting Companies, Inc.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESterling2004291_5-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESterling2004291-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199397_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199397-9">[9]</a></sup> Woods retained his position as president and CEO of ABC until December 1949, and was subsequently promoted to vice-chairman of the board before leaving ABC altogether on June 30, 1951.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECox200993_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECox200993-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Meanwhile, in August 1944, the West Coast division of the Blue Network, which owned <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a> radio station <a href="/wiki/KGO_(AM)" title="KGO (AM)">KGO</a>, bought <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> station KECA from <a href="/wiki/Earle_C._Anthony" title="Earle C. Anthony">Earle C. Anthony</a> for $800,000.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Both stations were then managed by Don Searle, the vice-president of the Blue Network's West Coast division.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Entry_into_television_.281945.E2.80.931949.29">Entry into television (1945–1949)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Broadcasting_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Entry into television (1945–1949)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The ABC Radio Network created its audience slowly. The network's acquisition of <a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">Detroit</a> radio station <a href="/wiki/WXYT_(AM)" title="WXYT (AM)">WXYZ</a> from KingTrendle Broadcasting in 1946 for a little less than $3 million (and which remained under ABC ownership until 1984),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESterling200488_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESterling200488-15">[15]</a></sup> allowed it to acquire several radio serials, including <i><a href="/wiki/Lone_Ranger" title="Lone Ranger">The Lone Ranger</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Sergeant_Preston_of_the_Yukon" class="mw-redirect" title="Sergeant Preston of the Yukon">Sergeant Preston</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Green_Hornet" title="Green Hornet">The Green Hornet</a></i>, which had originated on that station.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESterling200489_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESterling200489-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>ABC became an aggressive competitor to NBC and CBS when, continuing NBC Blue's traditions of public service, it aired symphony performances conducted by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Whiteman" title="Paul Whiteman">Paul Whiteman</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESterling2004291_5-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESterling2004291-5">[5]</a></sup> performances from the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Opera" title="Metropolitan Opera">Metropolitan Opera</a>, and jazz concerts aired as part of its broadcast of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Chamber_Music_Society_of_Lower_Basin_Street" title="The Chamber Music Society of Lower Basin Street">The Chamber Music Society of Lower Basin Street</a></i> announced by <a href="/wiki/Milton_Cross" title="Milton Cross">Milton Cross</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESterling2004292_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESterling2004292-17">[17]</a></sup> The network also became known for such suspenseful dramas as <i>Sherlock Holmes</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Gang_Busters" title="Gang Busters">Gang Busters</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Counterspy_(radio_series)" title="Counterspy (radio series)">Counterspy</a></i>, as well as several mid-afternoon youth-oriented programs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESterling2004292_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESterling2004292-17">[17]</a></sup> However, ABC made a name for itself by utilizing the practice of <a href="/wiki/Counterprogramming" title="Counterprogramming">counterprogramming</a>, with which it often placed shows of its own against the offerings of NBC and CBS, adopting the use of the <a href="/wiki/Magnetophon" title="Magnetophon">Magnetophon</a> tape recorder, brought to the U.S. from <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> after its conquest, to pre-record its programming.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESterling200489_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESterling200489-16">[16]</a></sup> With the help of the Magnetophon, ABC was able to provide its stars with greater freedom in terms of time, and also attract several big names, such as <a href="/wiki/Bing_Crosby" title="Bing Crosby">Bing Crosby</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESterling200489_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESterling200489-16">[16]</a></sup> at a time when NBC and CBS did not allow pre-taped shows.</p>
<p>While its radio network was undergoing reconstruction, ABC found it difficult to avoid falling behind on the new medium of <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">television</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993104_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993104-18">[18]</a></sup> To ensure a space, in 1947, ABC submitted five applications for television station licenses, one for each market where it owned and operated a radio station (New York City, Los Angeles, Chicago, San Francisco and Detroit).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray19976_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray19976-19">[19]</a></sup> These applications all requested for the stations to broadcast on <a href="/wiki/Very_high_frequency" title="Very high frequency">VHF</a> <a href="/wiki/North_American_broadcast_television_frequencies" class="mw-redirect" title="North American broadcast television frequencies">channel 7</a>, as <a href="/wiki/Frank_Marx" class="mw-redirect" title="Frank Marx">Frank Marx</a>, then ABC's vice-president of engineering, thought that the low-band VHF frequencies (corresponding to channels 2 through 6) would be requisitioned from broadcasting use and reallocated for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">U.S. Army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray19976_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray19976-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ABC television network made its debut on April 19, 1948, with WFIL-TV in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> (now <a href="/wiki/WPVI-TV" title="WPVI-TV">WPVI-TV</a>) becoming its first primary affiliate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECox200998_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECox200998-20">[20]</a></sup> The first program ever broadcast on the network was <i>On the Corner</i>, featuring satirist <a href="/wiki/Henry_Morgan_(comedian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry Morgan (comedian)">Henry Morgan</a>. Other stations carrying the initial broadcast were <a href="/wiki/WMAR-TV" title="WMAR-TV">WMAR-TV</a> in <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a>, <a href="/wiki/WJLA-TV" title="WJLA-TV">WMAL-TV</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> and <a href="/wiki/WNYW" title="WNYW">WABD</a>, the DuMont station in New York City, since ABC's New York station had yet to sign on.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The network's flagship owned-and-operated station, WJZ-TV in New York City (later re-called <a href="/wiki/WABC-TV" title="WABC-TV">WABC-TV</a>), signed on the air on August 10, 1948,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECox200998_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECox200998-20">[20]</a></sup> with its first broadcast running for two hours that evening.<sup id="cite_ref-billboard21081948_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-billboard21081948-22">[22]</a></sup> ABC's other owned-and-operated stations launched over the course of the next 13 months:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECox200998_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECox200998-20">[20]</a></sup> WENR-TV in Chicago signed on the air on September 17,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/WXYZ-TV" title="WXYZ-TV">WXYZ-TV</a> in Detroit went on the air on October 9, 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKiska200922_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKiska200922-24">[24]</a></sup> In October 1948, as a result of an influx of television station license applications that it had issued as well as a study it undertook on the use of the VHF spectrum for broadcasting purposes, the FCC implemented a <a href="/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission#The_freeze_of_1948" title="Federal Communications Commission">freeze on new station applications</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-freeze_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-freeze-25">[25]</a></sup> However, KGO-TV in San Francisco, which had received its license prior to the freeze, made its debut on May 5, 1949.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELasar200078_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELasar200078-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> On May 7, 1949, <i>Billboard</i> revealed that ABC had proposed an investment of $6.25 million, of which it would spend $2.5 million to convert 20 acres (80,937&#160;m<sup>2</sup>) of land in <a href="/wiki/Hollywood,_Los_Angeles" class="mw-redirect" title="Hollywood, Los Angeles">Hollywood</a> into what would become <a href="/wiki/The_Prospect_Studios" title="The Prospect Studios">The Prospect Studios</a>, and construct a transmitter on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Wilson_(California)" title="Mount Wilson (California)">Mount Wilson</a>, in anticipation of the launch of <a href="/wiki/KABC-TV" title="KABC-TV">KECA-TV</a>, which was scheduled to begin operations on August 1<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> (but would not actually sign on until September 16).<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the fall of 1949, ABC found itself in the position of an outsider, with less coverage than two of its competing networks, CBS and NBC, even though it was on par with them in some major cities and had a headstart over its third rival at the time, the <a href="/wiki/DuMont_Television_Network" title="DuMont Television Network">DuMont Television Network</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Freeze_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Freeze-31">[31]</a></sup> Before the freeze ended in 1952, there were only 108 existing television stations in the United States; a few major cities (such as <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>) had only two television stations, many other cities (such as <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a> and <a href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Louis, Missouri">St. Louis</a>) had only one, and still many others (such as <a href="/wiki/Denver" title="Denver">Denver</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portland,_Oregon" title="Portland, Oregon">Portland</a>) did not yet have any television service.<sup id="cite_ref-Freeze_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Freeze-31">[31]</a></sup> The result was an uneven transition period where television flourished in certain areas and network radio remained the sole source of broadcast entertainment and news in others.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="American_Broadcasting-Paramount_Theatres">American Broadcasting-Paramount Theatres</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Broadcasting_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: American Broadcasting-Paramount Theatres">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting-Paramount_Theatres" title="American Broadcasting-Paramount Theatres">American Broadcasting-Paramount Theatres</a></div>
<p>At the end of 1949, movie theater operator United Paramount Theatres (UPT) was forced by the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">U.S. Supreme Court</a> to become an independent entity, separating itself from <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Pictures" title="Paramount Pictures">Paramount Pictures</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELegrandLherminierMannoni1992422_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELegrandLherminierMannoni1992422-32">[32]</a></sup> For its part, ABC was on the verge of bankruptcy, with only five owned-and-operated stations and nine full-time affiliates.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinstein200628_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinstein200628-33">[33]</a></sup> Its revenues, which were related to advertising and were indexed compared to the number of listeners/viewers, failed to compensate for its heavy investments in purchasing and building stations. In 1951, a rumor even mentioned that the network would be sold to CBS.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray19976_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray19976-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuinlan197917_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuinlan197917-34">[34]</a></sup> In 1951, Noble held a 58% ownership stake in ABC,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993110_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993110-35">[35]</a></sup> giving him $5 million with which to prevent ABC from going bankrupt; as banks refused further credit,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199399_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199399-36">[36]</a></sup> that amount was obtained through a loan from the <a href="/wiki/Prudential_Financial" title="Prudential Financial">Prudential Insurance Company of America</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESterling200490_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESterling200490-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Leonard_Goldenson" title="Leonard Goldenson">Leonard Goldenson</a>, the president of UPT (which sought to diversify itself at the time), approached Noble in 1951 on a proposal for UPT to purchase ABC.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199399_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199399-36">[36]</a></sup> Noble received other offers, including one from CBS founder <a href="/wiki/William_S._Paley" title="William S. Paley">William S. Paley</a>; however, a merger with CBS would have forced that network to sell its New York City and Los Angeles stations at the very least.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993100_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993100-38">[38]</a></sup> Goldenson and Noble reached a tentative agreement in the late spring of 1951<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199399_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.93199399-36">[36]</a></sup> in which UPT would acquire ABC and turn it into a subsidiary of the company that would retain autonomy in its management.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993103_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993103-39">[39]</a></sup> On June 6, 1951, the tentative agreement was approved by UPT's board of directors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993103_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993103-39">[39]</a></sup> However, the transaction had to be approved by the FCC because of the presence of television networks and the recent separation between Paramount and UPT. Insofar as Paramount Pictures was already a shareholder in the DuMont Television Network, the FCC conducted a series of hearings to ensure whether Paramount was truly separated from United Paramount Theatres, and whether it was violating <a href="/wiki/Clayton_Antitrust_Act" title="Clayton Antitrust Act">antitrust laws</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993104_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993104-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1952, when the release of the FCC's <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sixth_Report_and_Order&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Sixth Report and Order (page does not exist)">Sixth Report and Order</a> announced the end of its freeze on new station license applications, among the issues the Commission was slated to address was whether to approve the UPT-ABC merger.<sup id="cite_ref-freeze_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-freeze-25">[25]</a></sup> One FCC Commissioner saw the possibility of ABC, funded by UPT, becoming a viable and competitive third television network.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993115_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993115-40">[40]</a></sup> On February 9, 1953, the FCC approved UPT's purchase of ABC in exchange for $25 million in shares.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993115_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993115-40">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The merged company, renamed <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting-Paramount_Theatres" title="American Broadcasting-Paramount Theatres">American Broadcasting-Paramount Theatres</a>, Inc. and headquartered in the <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Building" class="mw-redirect" title="Paramount Building">Paramount Building</a> at 1501 Broadway in Manhattan,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKahn200614_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKahn200614-42">[42]</a></sup> owned six AM and several FM radio stations, five television stations and 644 cinemas in 300 U.S. cities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESterling200490_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESterling200490-37">[37]</a></sup> To comply with FCC ownership restrictions in effect at the time that barred <a href="/wiki/Duopoly_(broadcasting)" title="Duopoly (broadcasting)">common ownership of two television stations in the same market</a>, UPT sold its Chicago television station, WBKB-TV, to CBS (which subsequently changed the station's call letters to <a href="/wiki/WBBM-TV" title="WBBM-TV">WBBM-TV</a>) for $6 million,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993102_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993102-43">[43]</a></sup> while it kept ABC's existing Chicago station, WENR-TV. The merged company acquired the WBKB call letters for channel 7, which would eventually become <a href="/wiki/WLS-TV" title="WLS-TV">WLS-TV</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoward1979141_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoward1979141-44">[44]</a></sup> Goldenson began to sell some of the older theaters to help finance the new television network.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESterling200490_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESterling200490-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>On May 1, 1953, ABC's New York City flagship stations – WJZ, WJZ-FM and WJZ-TV – changed their respective callsigns to <a href="/wiki/WABC_(AM)" title="WABC (AM)">WABC</a>, <a href="/wiki/WPLJ" title="WPLJ">WABC-FM</a> and <a href="/wiki/WABC-TV" title="WABC-TV">WABC-TV</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECox200994_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECox200994-45">[45]</a></sup> and moved their operations to facilities at 7 <a href="/wiki/66th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="66th Street (Manhattan)">West 66th Street</a>, one block away from <a href="/wiki/Central_Park" title="Central Park">Central Park</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESterling200490_37-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESterling200490-37">[37]</a></sup> The WABC call letters were previously used by the flagship station of CBS Radio (now <a href="/wiki/WCBS_(AM)" title="WCBS (AM)">WCBS (AM)</a>) until 1946. The WJZ calls would later be reassigned to <a href="/wiki/WJZ-TV" title="WJZ-TV">the then-ABC affiliate</a> in <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a> in 1959, in an historical nod to the fact that WJZ was originally established by the Baltimore station's owner at the time, <a href="/wiki/Westinghouse_Electric_(1886)" class="mw-redirect" title="Westinghouse Electric (1886)">Westinghouse</a>.</p>
<p>However, a problem emerged regarding the directions taken by ABC and UPT. In 1950, Noble appointed <a href="/wiki/Robert_Kintner" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Kintner">Robert Kintner</a> to be ABC's president while he himself served as its CEO, a position he would hold until his death in 1958.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993104_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993104-18">[18]</a></sup> Despite the promise of non-interference between ABC and UPT, Goldenson had to intervene in ABC's decisions because of financial problems and the FCC's long period of indecision. Goldenson added to the confusion when, in October 1954,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong1979110_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong1979110-46">[46]</a></sup> he proposed a merger between UPT and the DuMont Television Network, which was also mired in financial trouble.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993104_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993104-18">[18]</a></sup> As part of this merger, the network would have been renamed "ABC-DuMont" for five years, and DuMont would have received $5 million in cash, room on the schedule for existing DuMont programming, and guaranteed advertising time for DuMont Laboratories receivers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993104_18-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993104-18">[18]</a></sup> In addition, to comply with FCC ownership restrictions, it would have been required to sell either WABC-TV or DuMont owned-and-operated station WABD in the New York City market, as well as two other stations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993104_18-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993104-18">[18]</a></sup> The merged ABC-DuMont would have had the resources to compete with CBS and NBC.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong1979110_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong1979110-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Goldenson sought to develop the ABC television network by trying to convince local stations to agree to affiliate with the network.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993104_18-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993104-18">[18]</a></sup> In doing this, he contacted local entrepreneurs who owned television stations themselves, many of whom had previously invested in Paramount cinemas and had worked with him when he undertook the responsibility of restructuring United Paramount Theatres.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993104_18-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993104-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hollywood_begins_to_produce_television_series">Hollywood begins to produce television series</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Broadcasting_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Hollywood begins to produce television series">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>At the same time he made attempts to help grow ABC, Goldenson had been trying since mid-1953 to provide content for the network by contacting his old acquaintances in Hollywood, with whom he had worked when UPT was a subsidiary of Paramount Pictures.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993104_18-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993104-18">[18]</a></sup> ABC's merger with UPT led to the creation of relationships with Hollywood's film production studios, breaking a quarantine that had existed at that time between film and television,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaughmanAtack198358_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaughmanAtack198358-47">[47]</a></sup> the latter of which had previously been more connected to radio. ABC's flagship productions at the time were <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lone_Ranger_(TV_series)" title="The Lone Ranger (TV series)">The Lone Ranger</a></i>, based on the radio program of the same title, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Ozzie_and_Harriet" title="The Adventures of Ozzie and Harriet">The Adventures of Ozzie and Harriet</a></i>, the latter of which (at 14 seasons, running from 1952 to 1966) held the record for the longest-running prime time comedy in U.S. television history, until it was surpassed by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Simpsons" title="The Simpsons">The Simpsons</a></i> in 2003.</p>
<p>Goldenson's efforts paid off, and on October 27, 1954, the network launched a campaign ushering in the "New ABC", with productions from several studios, including <a href="/wiki/Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer" title="Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer">Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." title="Warner Bros.">Warner Bros.</a> and <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox" title="20th Century Fox">20th Century Fox</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoddy1992147.E2.80.93148_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoddy1992147.E2.80.93148-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Warner tried with mixed success to adapt some of its most successful films as ABC television series, and showcase these adaptations as part of the <a href="/wiki/Wheel_series" title="Wheel series">wheel series</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros._Presents" title="Warner Bros. Presents">Warner Bros. Presents</a></i>. Airing during the <a href="/wiki/1955%E2%80%9356_United_States_network_television_schedule" title="1955–56 United States network television schedule">1955–56 season</a>, it showcased television adaptations of the 1942 films <i><a href="/wiki/Kings_Row" title="Kings Row">Kings Row</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Casablanca_(film)" title="Casablanca (film)">Casablanca</a></i>; <i><a href="/wiki/Cheyenne_(1955_TV_series)" title="Cheyenne (1955 TV series)">Cheyenne</a></i> (adapted from the 1947 film <i><a href="/wiki/Wyoming_Kid" class="mw-redirect" title="Wyoming Kid">Wyoming Kid</a></i>); <i><a href="/wiki/Sugarfoot" title="Sugarfoot">Sugarfoot</a></i> (a remake of the 1954 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Boy_from_Oklahoma" title="The Boy from Oklahoma">The Boy from Oklahoma</a></i>); and <i><a href="/wiki/Maverick_(TV_series)" title="Maverick (TV series)">Maverick</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaughmanAtack198358_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaughmanAtack198358-47">[47]</a></sup> However, the most iconic of ABC's relationships with Hollywood producers was its agreement with <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">Walt Disney</a>; after the start of the network's bond with the Disney studio, James Lewis Baughman, who worked as a columnist at that time, observed that "at ABC's headquarters in New York, the secretaries [were now] wearing hats with <a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mouse" title="Mickey Mouse">Mickey Mouse</a> ears".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaughmanAtack198358_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaughmanAtack198358-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="First_bonds_with_Disney">First bonds with <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Company" title="The Walt Disney Company">Disney</a></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Broadcasting_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: First bonds with Disney">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Von_Braun_Discussing_Disney_Special.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Von_Braun_Discussing_Disney_Special.jpg/220px-Von_Braun_Discussing_Disney_Special.jpg" width="220" height="277" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Von_Braun_Discussing_Disney_Special.jpg/330px-Von_Braun_Discussing_Disney_Special.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/17/Von_Braun_Discussing_Disney_Special.jpg 2x" data-file-width="358" data-file-height="450" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Von_Braun_Discussing_Disney_Special.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Wernher_von_Braun" title="Wernher von Braun">Wernher von Braun</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Stuhlinger" title="Ernst Stuhlinger">Ernst Stuhlinger</a> in the episode "Mars and Beyond" (1957) from <i>Disneyland</i>, the first of many <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_anthology_television_series" title="Walt Disney anthology television series">Walt Disney anthology television series</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Walt Disney and his brother <a href="/wiki/Roy_Oliver_Disney" class="mw-redirect" title="Roy Oliver Disney">Roy</a> contacted Goldenson at the end of 1953<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993121.E2.80.93122_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993121.E2.80.93122-49">[49]</a></sup> for ABC to agree to finance part of the <a href="/wiki/Disneyland" title="Disneyland">Disneyland</a> project in exchange for producing a television program for the network.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith199824_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith199824-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-factbook_1997_2_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook_1997_2-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith1998152_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith1998152-52">[52]</a></sup> Walt wanted ABC to invest $500,000 and accrued a guarantee of $4.5 million in additional loans, a third of the budget intended for the park.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith199824_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith199824-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993123.E2.80.93124_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993123.E2.80.93124-53">[53]</a></sup> Around 1954, ABC agreed to finance Disneyland in exchange for the right to broadcast a new Sunday night program, <i>Disneyland</i>, which debuted on the network on October 27, 1954<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith199824_50-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith199824-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-factbook_1997_2_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook_1997_2-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith1998152_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith1998152-52">[52]</a></sup> as the first of many <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_anthology_television_series" title="Walt Disney anthology television series">anthology television programs</a> that Disney would broadcast over the course of the next 50 years.</p>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/Disneyland" title="Disneyland">Disneyland</a> opened on July 17, 1955, ABC aired a special live broadcast commemorating the park's first day of operation, <i><a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_anthology_television_series" title="Walt Disney anthology television series">Dateline: Disneyland</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith1998132_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith1998132-54">[54]</a></sup> Shortly thereafter, on October 3, 1955, a second regularly scheduled program produced by Disney made its debut, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mickey_Mouse_Club" title="The Mickey Mouse Club">The Mickey Mouse Club</a></i>, a children's program that aired Monday through Friday afternoons, which starred a group of 24 children known as the "Mouseketeers".<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_1997_2_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook_1997_2-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith1998363_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith1998363-55">[55]</a></sup> The two Disney programs made 1955 the year that the network was first profitable and as a station owner.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Affiliate_issues">Affiliate issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Broadcasting_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Affiliate issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>By 1954, all U.S. networks had regained control of their programming, with higher advertising revenues: ABC's revenue increased by 67% (earning $26 million), NBC's went up by 30% ($100 million) and CBS's rose by 44% ($117 million).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoddy1992160_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoddy1992160-57">[57]</a></sup> However that year, ABC had only 14 primary affiliates compared to the 74 that carried the majority of CBS programs and the 71 that were primarily affiliated with NBC. Most markets outside the largest ones were not large enough to support three full-time network affiliates. In some markets that were large enough for a third full-time affiliate, the only available commercial allocation was on the less-desirable <a href="/wiki/Ultra_high_frequency" title="Ultra high frequency">UHF</a> band. Until the <a href="/wiki/All-Channel_Receiver_Act" title="All-Channel Receiver Act">All-Channel Receiver Act</a> (passed by Congress in 1961) mandated the inclusion of UHF tuning, most viewers needed to purchase a converter to be able to watch UHF stations, and the signal quality was marginal at best even with a converter. Additionally, during the analog television era, UHF stations were not adequately receivable in rugged terrain. These factors made many prospective station owners skittish about investing in a UHF station, especially one that would have had to take on an affiliation with a weaker network.</p>
<p>As a result, with the exception of the largest markets, ABC was relegated to secondary status on one or both of the existing stations, usually via off-hours clearances (a notable exception during this time was WKST-TV in <a href="/wiki/Youngstown,_Ohio" title="Youngstown, Ohio">Youngstown, Ohio</a>, now <a href="/wiki/WYTV" title="WYTV">WYTV</a>, despite the small size of the surrounding market and its close proximity to <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a> even decades before <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Youngstown,_Ohio#Black_Monday" title="Economy of Youngstown, Ohio">the city's economic collapse</a>). According to Goldenson, this meant that an hour of ABC programming reported five times lower viewership than its competitors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993116_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993116-58">[58]</a></sup> However, the network's intake of money at the time would allow it to accelerate its content production. Still, ABC's limited reach would continue to hobble it for the next two decades; several smaller markets would not grow large enough to support a full-time ABC affiliate until the 1960s, with some very small markets having to wait as late as the 1980s or even the advent of <a href="/wiki/Digital_terrestrial_television" title="Digital terrestrial television">digital television</a> in the 2000s, which allowed stations like <a href="/wiki/WTRF-TV" title="WTRF-TV">WTRF-TV</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wheeling,_West_Virginia" title="Wheeling, West Virginia">Wheeling, West Virginia</a> to begin airing ABC programming on a <a href="/wiki/Digital_subchannel" title="Digital subchannel">digital subchannel</a> after airing the network's programs outside of recommended timeslots decades before.</p>
<p>The DuMont Television Network ceased broadcasting on September 15, 1955,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong1979110_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong1979110-46">[46]</a></sup> and went bankrupt the next year. ABC then found itself as the third U.S. television network, dubbed the "little third network",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith1998151_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith1998151-59">[59]</a></sup> but still continued to look for successful programming. That same year, Kintner was forced to resign due to disagreements between Noble and Goldenson,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993104_18-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993104-18">[18]</a></sup> a consequence of Goldenson's many interventions in ABC's management.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWittebols200422_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWittebols200422-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Counterprogramming:_successful.2C_but_criticized">Counterprogramming: successful, but criticized</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Broadcasting_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Counterprogramming: successful, but criticized">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:American_Bandstand_50th_reunion.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/American_Bandstand_50th_reunion.jpg/220px-American_Bandstand_50th_reunion.jpg" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/American_Bandstand_50th_reunion.jpg/330px-American_Bandstand_50th_reunion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/American_Bandstand_50th_reunion.jpg/440px-American_Bandstand_50th_reunion.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1914" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:American_Bandstand_50th_reunion.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In 2002, dancers and other cast members from the 32-year run of <i>American Bandstand</i> reunited with host <a href="/wiki/Dick_Clark" title="Dick Clark">Dick Clark</a> to celebrate the 50th anniversary of the show's local television debut.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>It was not until the late 1950s that the ABC network became a serious contender to NBC and CBS, and this was in large part due to the diverse range of programming that met the expectations of the public, such as westerns and detective series. Despite an almost 500% increase in advertising revenues between 1953 and 1958, the network only had a national reach of between 10% and 18% of the total U.S. population, as it still had relatively fewer affiliates than NBC and CBS.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoddy1992147_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoddy1992147-61">[61]</a></sup> In 1957, ABC Entertainment president Ollie Treiz discovered that the locally produced variety show <i>Bandstand</i> had pulled very strong ratings in the Philadelphia market on WFIL-TV; Treiz ultimately negotiated a deal to take the show national, under the revised title <i><a href="/wiki/American_Bandstand" title="American Bandstand">American Bandstand</a></i>;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993161.E2.80.93163_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993161.E2.80.93163-62">[62]</a></sup> the show quickly became a social phenomenon by presenting new musical talent and dances to America's youth<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993161.E2.80.93163_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993161.E2.80.93163-62">[62]</a></sup> and helped make a star out of its host, <a href="/wiki/Dick_Clark" title="Dick Clark">Dick Clark</a>.</p>
<p>On September 3, 1958, the <i>Disneyland</i> anthology series was retitled <i>Walt Disney Presents</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith1998152_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith1998152-52">[52]</a></sup> as it became disassociated with the theme park of the same name. The movement in westerns, which ABC is credited for having started, represented a fifth of all primetime series on American television in January 1959, at which point detective shows were beginning to rise in popularity as well.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaughmanAtack198359_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaughmanAtack198359-63">[63]</a></sup> ABC requested additional productions from Disney.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECotter199766_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECotter199766-64">[64]</a></sup> In late 1958, <a href="/wiki/Desilu_Productions" title="Desilu Productions">Desilu Productions</a> pitched its detective series <i><a href="/wiki/The_Untouchables_(1959_TV_series)" title="The Untouchables (1959 TV series)">The Untouchables</a></i> to CBS; after that network rejected the show because of its use of violence, Desilu then presented it to ABC, which agreed to pick up the show,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaughmanAtack198363_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaughmanAtack198363-65">[65]</a></sup> and debuted <i>The Untouchables</i> in April 1959. The series went on to quickly become "immensely popular".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaughmanAtack198363_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaughmanAtack198363-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>These kinds of programs presented ABC with an image of the "philosophy of counterprogramming against its competitors", offering a strong lineup of programs that contrasted with those seen on its rival networks,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECotter199766_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECotter199766-64">[64]</a></sup> which helped Goldenson give the network a continuum between film and television.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaughmanAtack198360_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaughmanAtack198360-66">[66]</a></sup> ABC's western series (as well as series such as the actioner <i><a href="/wiki/Zorro_(1957_TV_series)" title="Zorro (1957 TV series)">Zorro</a></i>) went up against and defeated the variety shows aired by NBC and CBS in the fall of 1957, and its detective shows did the same in the fall of 1959. To captivate the network's audiences, short 66-minute series were scheduled a half-hour before their hour-long competition.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaughmanAtack198360_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaughmanAtack198360-66">[66]</a></sup> In May 1961, <i><a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)">Life</a></i> criticized the public enthusiasm and sponsorship for these types of shows at the expense of news programming and denounced an unofficial law "replacing the good programs with the bad ones".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaughmanAtack198363.E2.80.9364_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaughmanAtack198363.E2.80.9364-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transition_to_color_.281960s.29">Transition to color (1960s)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Broadcasting_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Transition to color (1960s)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the 1960s, ABC continued on the same path that it began to take in the mid-1950s, by consolidating the network as part of its effort to gain loyalty from the public. The network's finances improved and allowed it to invest in other properties and programming. In May 1960, ABC purchased Chicago radio station <a href="/wiki/WLS_(AM)" title="WLS (AM)">WLS</a>, which had shared airtime with WENR since the 1920s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChilders20088_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChilders20088-68">[68]</a></sup> This acquisition allowed ABC to consolidate its presence in the market. On May 9, 1960, WLS launched a new lineup consisting of ABC Radio programming.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> In 1960, Canadian entrepreneur <a href="/wiki/John_Bassett" title="John Bassett">John Bassett</a>, who was trying to establish a television station in <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a>, sought the help of ABC to launch the station.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993232_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993232-70">[70]</a></sup> Leonard Goldenson agreed to acquire a 25% interest in <a href="/wiki/CFTO-DT" title="CFTO-DT">CFTO-TV</a>; however, legislation by the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Radio-Television_and_Telecommunications_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="Canadian Radio-Television and Telecommunications Commission">Canadian Radio-Television Commission</a> prohibited ABC's involvement, resulting in the company withdrawing from the project before the station's launch.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993232_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993232-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Children.27s_programming_and_the_debut_of_ABC_Sports_.281960.E2.80.931965.29">Children's programming and the debut of ABC Sports (1960–1965)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Broadcasting_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Children's programming and the debut of ABC Sports (1960–1965)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ABC_color_logo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/ABC_color_logo.jpg/220px-ABC_color_logo.jpg" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5d/ABC_color_logo.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="262" data-file-height="208" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ABC_color_logo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The "ABC Circle" logo, designed by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Rand" title="Paul Rand">Paul Rand</a>, was introduced in a 1962 identification card.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 1960s would be marked by the rise of family-oriented series in an attempt by ABC to counterprogram its established competitors, but the decade was also marked by the network's gradual transition to color.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993251_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993251-71">[71]</a></sup> On September 30, 1960, ABC premiered <i><a href="/wiki/The_Flintstones" title="The Flintstones">The Flintstones</a></i>, another example of counterprogramming; although the animated series from <a href="/wiki/William_Hanna" title="William Hanna">William Hanna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Barbera" title="Joseph Barbera">Joseph Barbera</a> was filmed in color from the beginning, it was initially broadcast in <a href="/wiki/Black-and-white" class="mw-redirect" title="Black-and-white">black-and-white</a>, as ABC had not made the necessary technical upgrades to broadcast its programming in color at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStabileHarrison200376_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStabileHarrison200376-72">[72]</a></sup> <i>The Flintstones</i> allowed ABC to present a novelty, that of prime-time animated programming, but it also allowed the network to begin filling the hole opened by the conclusion of the Disney partnership by carrying family-oriented programming from other producers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStabileHarrison200376_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStabileHarrison200376-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1959, Walt Disney Productions, having improved its financial situation, had purchased ABC's shares in the Disneyland theme park for $7.5 million<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith199824_50-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith199824-50">[50]</a></sup> and initiated discussions to renew ABC's television contract for <i>Walt Disney Presents</i>, which was due to expire in 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993251_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993251-71">[71]</a></sup> Walt Disney was approached by NBC to produce color broadcasts of his <a href="/wiki/Anthology_series" title="Anthology series">anthology series</a> (which would be renamed <i>Walt Disney's Wonderful World of Color</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993251_71-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993251-71">[71]</a></sup> Goldenson said ABC could not counter the offer, because the network did not have the technical and financial resources to carry the program in the format.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993251_71-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993251-71">[71]</a></sup> As a result, ABC and Disney's first television collaboration ended in 1961<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith199824_50-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith199824-50">[50]</a></sup> (the network would resume its relationship with Disney in 1985, when the anthology series returned to the network for a three-season run as the <i>Disney Sunday Movie</i> until it lost the rights to NBC again in 1988; the Disney anthology series would return to ABC in 1996, following the company's purchase of the future Capital Cities/ABC, as <i>The Wonderful World of Disney</i>).</p>
<p>However, in 1961, ABC continued with its niche in animated series with <i><a href="/wiki/Calvin_and_the_Colonel" title="Calvin and the Colonel">Calvin and the Colonel</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Matty%27s_Funday_Funnies" title="Matty's Funday Funnies">Matty's Funday Funnies</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Top_Cat" title="Top Cat">Top Cat</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bugs_Bunny_Show" title="The Bugs Bunny Show">The Bugs Bunny Show</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStabileHarrison200376_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStabileHarrison200376-72">[72]</a></sup> the latter of which showcased classic <i><a href="/wiki/Looney_Tunes" title="Looney Tunes">Looney Tunes</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Merrie_Melodies" title="Merrie Melodies">Merrie Melodies</a></i> shorts.</p>
<p>Always in search of new programs that would help it compete with NBC and CBS, ABC's management believed that sports could be a major catalyst in improving the network's market share.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993195_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993195-73">[73]</a></sup> On April 29, 1961, ABC debuted <i><a href="/wiki/Wide_World_of_Sports_(U.S._TV_series)" title="Wide World of Sports (U.S. TV series)">Wide World of Sports</a></i>, an anthology series created by <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Scherick" title="Edgar Scherick">Edgar Scherick</a> through his company Sports Programs, Inc. and produced by a young <a href="/wiki/Roone_Arledge" title="Roone Arledge">Roone Arledge</a> which featured a different sporting event each broadcast.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFord2004466_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFord2004466-75">[75]</a></sup> ABC purchased Sports Programs, Inc. in exchange for shares in the company,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFord2004468_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFord2004468-76">[76]</a></sup> leading it to become the future core of <a href="/wiki/ESPN_on_ABC" title="ESPN on ABC">ABC Sports</a>, with Arledge as the executive producer of that division's shows.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993182_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993182-77">[77]</a></sup> <i>Wide World of Sports</i>, in particular, was not merely devoted to a single sport, but rather to generally all sporting events.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993195_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993195-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Due to pressure from film studios wanting to increase their production, as the major networks began airing theatrically released films,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993252_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993252-78">[78]</a></sup> ABC joined CBS and NBC in broadcasting films on Sunday nights in 1962, with the launch of the <i><a href="/wiki/ABC_Sunday_Night_Movie" class="mw-redirect" title="ABC Sunday Night Movie">ABC Sunday Night Movie</a></i>, which debuted a year behind its competitors and was initially presented in black-and-white.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993252_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993252-78">[78]</a></sup> Despite a significant increase in viewership (with its audience share having increased to 33% from the 15% share it had in 1953), ABC remained in third place; the company had a total revenue of $15.5 million, a third of the revenue pulled in by CBS at the same period.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993252_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993252-78">[78]</a></sup> To catch up, ABC followed up <i>The Flintstones</i> with another animated series from <a href="/wiki/Hanna-Barbera" title="Hanna-Barbera">Hanna-Barbera</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Jetsons" title="The Jetsons">The Jetsons</a></i>, which debuted on September 23, 1962 as the first television series to be broadcast in color on the network.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> On April 1, 1963, ABC debuted the <a href="/wiki/Soap_opera" title="Soap opera">soap opera</a> <i><a href="/wiki/General_Hospital" title="General Hospital">General Hospital</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993369_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993369-80">[80]</a></sup> which would go on to become the television network's long-running entertainment program. That year also saw the premiere of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Fugitive_(TV_series)" title="The Fugitive (TV series)">The Fugitive</a></i> (on September 17),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993241_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993241-81">[81]</a></sup> a drama series centering on a man on the run after being accused of committing a murder he did not commit.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/1964%E2%80%9365_United_States_network_television_schedule" title="1964–65 United States network television schedule">1964–65 season</a> was marked by the debuts of several classic series including <i><a href="/wiki/Bewitched" title="Bewitched">Bewitched</a></i> (on September 17)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993243_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993243-82">[82]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Addams_Family_(1964_TV_series)" title="The Addams Family (1964 TV series)">The Addams Family</a></i> (on September 18). Arledge's success with acquiring prime sports content was confirmed in 1964 when he was appointed vice-president of ABC Sports.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="New_regulations_and_the_radio_network.27s_recovery_.281966.E2.80.931969.29">New regulations and the radio network's recovery (1966–1969)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Broadcasting_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: New regulations and the radio network's recovery (1966–1969)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MINIATURE_RHODODENDRONS_BLOOM_IN_FRONT_OF_THE_AMERICAN_BROADCASTING_BUILDING_-_NARA_-_551665.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/MINIATURE_RHODODENDRONS_BLOOM_IN_FRONT_OF_THE_AMERICAN_BROADCASTING_BUILDING_-_NARA_-_551665.jpg/220px-MINIATURE_RHODODENDRONS_BLOOM_IN_FRONT_OF_THE_AMERICAN_BROADCASTING_BUILDING_-_NARA_-_551665.jpg" width="220" height="325" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/MINIATURE_RHODODENDRONS_BLOOM_IN_FRONT_OF_THE_AMERICAN_BROADCASTING_BUILDING_-_NARA_-_551665.jpg/330px-MINIATURE_RHODODENDRONS_BLOOM_IN_FRONT_OF_THE_AMERICAN_BROADCASTING_BUILDING_-_NARA_-_551665.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/MINIATURE_RHODODENDRONS_BLOOM_IN_FRONT_OF_THE_AMERICAN_BROADCASTING_BUILDING_-_NARA_-_551665.jpg/440px-MINIATURE_RHODODENDRONS_BLOOM_IN_FRONT_OF_THE_AMERICAN_BROADCASTING_BUILDING_-_NARA_-_551665.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2029" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MINIATURE_RHODODENDRONS_BLOOM_IN_FRONT_OF_THE_AMERICAN_BROADCASTING_BUILDING_-_NARA_-_551665.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
During this period, ABC moved its corporate headquarters to 1330 <a href="/wiki/Avenue_of_the_Americas" class="mw-redirect" title="Avenue of the Americas">Avenue of the Americas</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>It was not until the <a href="/wiki/1965%E2%80%9366_United_States_network_television_schedule" title="1965–66 United States network television schedule">1965–66 season</a> that color became the dominant format for the three broadcast television networks. ABC, meanwhile, remained in third place and still needed money to grow itself into a major competitor. However, ABC's issues with its transition to color became secondary compared to the network's financial problems; in 1964, the network found itself, as Goldenson later wrote in the 1991 book <i>Beating the Odds: The Untold Story Behind the Rise of ABC</i>, "in the middle of a war [where] the battlefield was <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street" title="Wall Street">Wall Street</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993250_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993250-84">[84]</a></sup> Many companies sought to take over ABC, including <a href="/wiki/Norton_Simon" title="Norton Simon">Norton Simon</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993252_78-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993252-78">[78]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gulf_and_Western_Industries" title="Gulf and Western Industries">Gulf and Western Industries</a>, <a href="/wiki/ITT_Corporation" title="ITT Corporation">International Telephone and Telegraph</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993258_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993258-85">[85]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Litton_Industries" title="Litton Industries">Litton Industries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993260_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993260-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1965, the corporate entity, <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting-Paramount_Theatres" title="American Broadcasting-Paramount Theatres">American Broadcasting-Paramount Theatres</a>, was renamed as the American Broadcasting Companies,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKahn2006284_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKahn2006284-87">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> while its cinema division became <a href="/wiki/ABC_Theatres" class="mw-redirect" title="ABC Theatres">ABC Theatres</a>; its recording division was renamed <a href="/wiki/ABC_Records" title="ABC Records">ABC Records</a> in 1966.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> In December of that year, the ABC television network premiered <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dating_Game" title="The Dating Game">The Dating Game</a></i>, a pioneer series in <a href="/wiki/Dating_game_show" title="Dating game show">its genre</a>, which was a reworking of the <a href="/wiki/Blind_date" title="Blind date">blind date</a> concept in which a suitor selected one of three contestants sight unseen based on the answers to selected questions. This was followed up in July 1966 by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Newlywed_Game" title="The Newlywed Game">The Newlywed Game</a></i>, featuring three recently married couples who guessed the responses to their partner's questions (some of which were fairly risque). As ABC began to outgrow its facilities at 7 West 66th Street, Goldenson found a new headquarters for ABC in a 44 story building located at 1330 <a href="/wiki/Avenue_of_the_Americas" class="mw-redirect" title="Avenue of the Americas">Avenue of the Americas</a> in Manhattan, at the corner of 54th Street<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993243.E2.80.93244_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993243.E2.80.93244-90">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Billboard24101964_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard24101964-91">[91]</a></sup> (now occupied by <i><a href="/wiki/Financial_Times" title="Financial Times">The Financial Times</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s New York office). This operation allowed for the conversion of the premises at 66th Street into production facilities for television and radio programs.<sup id="cite_ref-Billboard24101964_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard24101964-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>On December 7, 1965, Goldenson announced a merger proposal with ITT to ABC management; the two companies agreed to the deal on April 27, 1966.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993260_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993260-86">[86]</a></sup> The FCC approved the merger on December 21, 1966; however, the previous day (December 20), <a href="/wiki/Donald_F._Turner" title="Donald F. Turner">Donald F. Turner</a>, head <a href="/wiki/Antitrust" class="mw-redirect" title="Antitrust">antitrust</a> regulator for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">United States Department of Justice</a>, expressed doubts related to such issues as the emerging <a href="/wiki/Cable_television" title="Cable television">cable television</a> market,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993262_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993262-92">[92]</a></sup> and concerns over the journalistic integrity of ABC and how it could be influenced by the overseas ownership of ITT.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993261_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993261-93">[93]</a></sup> ITT management promised that the company would allow ABC to retain autonomy in the publishing business.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993262_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993262-92">[92]</a></sup> The merger was suspended, and a complaint was filed by the Department of Justice in July 1967, with ITT going to trial in October 1967; the merger was officially canceled after the trial's conclusion on January 1, 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993263_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993263-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
