<p>On January 12, 1966, ABC replaced <i>The Adventures of Ozzie and Harriet</i> with <i><a href="/wiki/Batman_(TV_series)" title="Batman (TV series)">Batman</a></i>, an action series based on the <a href="/wiki/DC_Comics" title="DC Comics">DC Comics</a> series starring <a href="/wiki/Adam_West" title="Adam West">Adam West</a> that was known for its kitschy style.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993247_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993247-95">[95]</a></sup> In 1967, WLS radio CEO Ralph Beaudin was appointed as the president of ABC Radio.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECox200998.E2.80.9399_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECox200998.E2.80.9399-96">[96]</a></sup> Under his leadership, ABC Radio was divided into four "networks" devoted to different types of programming: news, informative series, <a href="/wiki/Popular_music" title="Popular music">pop music</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Talk_show" title="Talk show">talk shows</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECox200998.E2.80.9399_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECox200998.E2.80.9399-96">[96]</a></sup> Two other networks were later created to provide <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">rock music</a> and traffic reporting.</p>
<p>In 1968, ABC took advantage of new FCC ownership regulations that allowed broadcasting companies to own a maximum of seven radio stations nationwide in order to purchase <a href="/wiki/Houston" title="Houston">Houston</a> radio stations <a href="/wiki/KXYZ" title="KXYZ">KXYZ</a> and <a href="/wiki/KHMX" title="KHMX">KXYZ-FM</a> for $1 million in shares and $1.5 million in bonds.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> That year, Roone Arledge was named president of ABC Sports; the company also founded ABC Pictures, a film production company which released its first picture that year, the <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Nelson" title="Ralph Nelson">Ralph Nelson</a>-directed <i><a href="/wiki/Charly" title="Charly">Charly</a></i>. It was renamed <a href="/wiki/ABC_Motion_Pictures" class="mw-redirect" title="ABC Motion Pictures">ABC Motion Pictures</a> in 1979; the unit was dissolved in 1985.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> The studio also operated two subsidiaries, Palomar Pictures International and Selmur Pictures. In July 1968, ABC continued its acquisitions in the amusement parks sector with the opening of ABC Marine World in <a href="/wiki/Redwood_City,_California" title="Redwood City, California">Redwood City, California</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993424_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993424-99">[99]</a></sup> that park was sold in 1972 and demolished in 1986, with the land that occupied the park later becoming home to the headquarters of <a href="/wiki/Oracle_Corporation" title="Oracle Corporation">Oracle Corporation</a>.</p>
<p>In July 1968, ABC Radio launched a special programming project for its FM stations, which was spearheaded by Allen Shaw,<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> a former program manager at <a href="/wiki/WCFL_(AM)" title="WCFL (AM)">WCFL</a> in Chicago<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalker2004106.E2.80.93107_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker2004106.E2.80.93107-101">[101]</a></sup> who was approached by ABC Radio president Harold L. Neal to develop a format to compete with the new <a href="/wiki/Progressive_rock" title="Progressive rock">progressive rock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Disk_jockey" class="mw-redirect" title="Disk jockey">DJ</a>-helmed stations.<sup id="cite_ref-bb30111968-55_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bb30111968-55-102">[102]</a></sup> The new concept called "LOVE Radio", which featured a limited selection of music genres, was launched on ABC's seven owned-and-operated FM stations in late November 1968; the concept replaced nearly all of the programming provided by these stations; however, several affiliates (such as KXYZ) retained the majority of their content.<sup id="cite_ref-bb30111968-55_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bb30111968-55-102">[102]</a></sup> In August 1970, Shaw announced that ABC FM's music choice policy should be reviewed to allow listeners access to many styles of music.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the television side, in September 1969, ABC launched the <i><a href="/wiki/ABC_Movie_of_the_Week" title="ABC Movie of the Week">Movie of the Week</a></i>, a weekly showcase aimed at capitalizing on the growing success of <a href="/wiki/Television_film" title="Television film">made-for-TV movies</a> since the early 1960s. The <i>Movie of the Week</i> broadcast feature-length dramatic films directed by such talented filmmakers as <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Spelling" title="Aaron Spelling">Aaron Spelling</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Wolper" class="mw-redirect" title="David Wolper">David Wolper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steven_Spielberg" title="Steven Spielberg">Steven Spielberg</a> (the latter of whom gained early success through the showcase for his 1971 film <i><a href="/wiki/Duel_(1971_film)" title="Duel (1971 film)">Duel</a></i>) that were produced on an average budget of $400,000–$450,000.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKarol20083_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKarol20083-104">[104]</a></sup> Hits for the television network during the late 1960s and early 1970s included <i><a href="/wiki/The_Courtship_of_Eddie%27s_Father" title="The Courtship of Eddie's Father">The Courtship of Eddie's Father</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Brady_Bunch" title="The Brady Bunch">The Brady Bunch</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Partridge_Family" title="The Partridge Family">The Partridge Family</a></i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Success_in_television_.281971.E2.80.931980.29">Success in television (1971–1980)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Broadcasting_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Success in television (1971–1980)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ABC_Television_Mobile_Unit_1976.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/ABC_Television_Mobile_Unit_1976.JPG/220px-ABC_Television_Mobile_Unit_1976.JPG" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/ABC_Television_Mobile_Unit_1976.JPG/330px-ABC_Television_Mobile_Unit_1976.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/ABC_Television_Mobile_Unit_1976.JPG/440px-ABC_Television_Mobile_Unit_1976.JPG 2x" data-file-width="739" data-file-height="566" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ABC_Television_Mobile_Unit_1976.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Publicity photo of the mobile studios used by ABC in 1976.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Carradine_as_Caine_from_Kung_Fu_-_c._1972%E2%80%931975.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/David_Carradine_as_Caine_from_Kung_Fu_-_c._1972%E2%80%931975.jpg/220px-David_Carradine_as_Caine_from_Kung_Fu_-_c._1972%E2%80%931975.jpg" width="220" height="283" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/David_Carradine_as_Caine_from_Kung_Fu_-_c._1972%E2%80%931975.jpg/330px-David_Carradine_as_Caine_from_Kung_Fu_-_c._1972%E2%80%931975.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/David_Carradine_as_Caine_from_Kung_Fu_-_c._1972%E2%80%931975.jpg/440px-David_Carradine_as_Caine_from_Kung_Fu_-_c._1972%E2%80%931975.jpg 2x" data-file-width="495" data-file-height="637" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Carradine_as_Caine_from_Kung_Fu_-_c._1972%E2%80%931975.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/David_Carradine" title="David Carradine">David Carradine</a>, star of <i><a href="/wiki/Kung_Fu_(TV_series)" title="Kung Fu (TV series)">Kung Fu</a></i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the early 1970s, ABC completed its transition to color; the decade as a whole would mark a turning point for ABC, as it began to pass CBS and NBC in the ratings to become the first place network.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993366_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993366-105">[105]</a></sup> It also began to use behavioral and demographic data to better determine what types of sponsors to sell advertising slots to and provide programming that would appeal towards certain audiences.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoltPerren200975.E2.80.9376_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoltPerren200975.E2.80.9376-106">[106]</a></sup> ABC's gains in audience share were greatly helped by the fact that several smaller markets had grown large enough to allow full-time affiliations from all three networks.</p>
<p>In 1970, ABC debuted <i><a href="/wiki/Monday_Night_Football" title="Monday Night Football">Monday Night Football</a></i> as part of its Monday prime time schedule;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993210_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993210-107">[107]</a></sup> the program became a hit for the network and served as the <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">National Football League</a> (NFL)'s premier game of the week until 2006, when <i><a href="/wiki/NBC_Sunday_Night_Football" title="NBC Sunday Night Football">Sunday Night Football</a></i>, which moved to NBC that year as part of a broadcast deal that in turn saw <i>MNF</i> move to <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">ESPN</a>, took over as the league's marquee game. According to Goldenson, <i>Monday Night Football</i> helped earn ABC regularly score an audience share of 15%–16%; ABC Sports managed the budget for the Monday night time slot to reallocate the weekly budget for ABC's prime time schedule to just six days, as opposed to seven on competing networks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993211_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993211-108">[108]</a></sup> 1970 also saw the premieres of several soap operas including the long-running <i><a href="/wiki/All_My_Children" title="All My Children">All My Children</a></i>, which ran on the network for 41 years.</p>
<p>In 1970, the FCC voted to pass the <a href="/wiki/Financial_Interest_and_Syndication_Rules" title="Financial Interest and Syndication Rules">Financial Interest and Syndication Rules</a>, a set of regulations aimed at preventing the major networks from monopolizing the broadcast landscape by barring them from owning any of the prime time programming that they broadcast.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroteauHoynes200685_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroteauHoynes200685-109">[109]</a></sup> In 1972, the new rules resulted in the company's decision to split ABC Films into two separate companies: the existing Worldvision Enterprises, which would produce and distribute programming for U.S. syndication, and <a href="/wiki/ABC_Circle_Films" class="mw-redirect" title="ABC Circle Films">ABC Circle Films</a> as a production unit.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993231_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993231-110">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown1977300_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown1977300-111">[111]</a></sup> Worldvision was sold to a consortium of ABC executives for nearly $10 million.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown1977300_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown1977300-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 1970, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a> passed the <a href="/wiki/Public_Health_Cigarette_Smoking_Act" title="Public Health Cigarette Smoking Act">Public Health Cigarette Smoking Act</a> which banned <a href="/wiki/Cigarette" title="Cigarette">cigarette</a> advertising from all television and radio networks, including ABC, when it took effect on January 2, 1971.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Citing limited profitability of its cinemas, ABC Great States, the Central West division of ABC Theatres, was sold to Henry Plitt in 1974.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993231_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993231-110">[110]</a></sup> On January 17, 1972, <a href="/wiki/Elton_Rule" title="Elton Rule">Elton Rule</a> was named President and <a href="/wiki/Chief_Operating_Officer" class="mw-redirect" title="Chief Operating Officer">Chief Operating Officer</a> of ABC a few months after Goldenson reduced his role in the company after suffering a <a href="/wiki/Heart_attack" class="mw-redirect" title="Heart attack">heart attack</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuinlan197917_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuinlan197917-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the early 1970s, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Eisner" title="Michael Eisner">Michael Eisner</a>, who joined ABC in 1966, became the network's program development manager.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993347_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993347-113">[113]</a></sup> He helped bring about ideas for many series including <i><a href="/wiki/Happy_Days" title="Happy Days">Happy Days</a></i> (which originated as a segment on the anthology series <i><a href="/wiki/Love,_American_Style" title="Love, American Style">Love, American Style</a></i>),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993347_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993347-113">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993348_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993348-114">[114]</a></sup> as well as several soap operas; however, Eisner's main credit at ABC was for developing youth-oriented programming.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993349_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993349-115">[115]</a></sup> He was responsible for reacquiring the rights to the Looney Tunes-Merrie Melodies library, bringing the shorts back to ABC after spending several years on CBS, as well as developing <i><a href="/wiki/The_Jackson_5ive_(TV_series)" title="The Jackson 5ive (TV series)">The Jackson 5ive</a></i> animated series and a series about <a href="/wiki/The_Osmonds" title="The Osmonds">the Osmonds</a>, and greenlighting <i><a href="/wiki/Super_Friends" title="Super Friends">Super Friends</a></i>, based on DC Comics' <a href="/wiki/Justice_League" title="Justice League">Justice League of America</a> series<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993347_113-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993347-113">[113]</a></sup> He also laid ground-work for the development of educational children's programming (predating the 1990 Congressional passage of the <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_Television_Act" title="Children's Television Act">Children's Television Act</a>) through interstitials such as <i><a href="/wiki/Time_for_Timer" title="Time for Timer">Time for Timer</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bod_Squad" title="The Bod Squad">The Bod Squad</a></i>, and, perhaps most famously, <i><a href="/wiki/Schoolhouse_Rock!" title="Schoolhouse Rock!">Schoolhouse Rock!</a></i>. Eisner left ABC in 1976 to become president of Paramount Pictures;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELegrandLherminierMannoni1992692_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELegrandLherminierMannoni1992692-116">[116]</a></sup> he would later become the President of ABC's eventual parent company, Disney.</p>
<p>In the spring of 1975, Fred Pierce, the newly appointed president of ABC Television, convinced <a href="/wiki/Fred_Silverman" title="Fred Silverman">Fred Silverman</a> to become the first president and director of programming of the independent television production subsidiary ABC Entertainment, created from the network's namesake programming division.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVaneGross1994181_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVaneGross1994181-117">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECastlemanPodrazik1982266.E2.80.93268_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECastlemanPodrazik1982266.E2.80.93268-118">[118]</a></sup> In 1974, ABC premiered the detective series <i><a href="/wiki/S.W.A.T._(TV_series)" title="S.W.A.T. (TV series)">S.W.A.T.</a></i> That same year, the network made the decision to compete with NBC's <a href="/wiki/Breakfast_television" title="Breakfast television">morning news-talk program</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Today_(U.S._TV_program)" title="Today (U.S. TV program)">Today</a></i>. Its first attempt at such competition was <i><a href="/wiki/AM_America" title="AM America">AM America</a></i>; however, that show's success was not straightforward.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993353_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993353-119">[119]</a></sup> One of its affiliates, <a href="/wiki/WCVB-TV" title="WCVB-TV">WCVB-TV</a> premiered morning show <i>Good Day!</i>. First premiering in 1973 as <i>Good Morning!</i>, it was groundbreaking for being entirely produced on the road and broadcasting from locations outside of the Boston area. Also, in the summer of 1975, ABC discovered that its <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland</a> affiliate <a href="/wiki/WEWS-TV" title="WEWS-TV">WEWS-TV</a> was producing its own morning program <i><a href="/wiki/The_Morning_Exchange" title="The Morning Exchange">The Morning Exchange</a></i>, which debuted in 1972 and was now locally pre-empting <i>AM America</i>; it was the first morning show to utilize a set modeled after a <a href="/wiki/Living_room" title="Living room">living room</a>, and established a concept now commonplace among network morning shows in which news and weather updates were featured at the top and bottom of each hour. Discovering that their formats seemed to appeal to their viewers,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993353_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993353-119">[119]</a></sup> the network became the first to adopt them for a new national morning show, <i><a href="/wiki/Good_Morning_America" title="Good Morning America">Good Morning America</a></i>, which debuted on November 3, 1975.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993353_119-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993353-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 1970s were highlighted by several successful comedy, fantasy, action and superhero-themed series for the network including <i><a href="/wiki/Kung_Fu_(TV_series)" title="Kung Fu (TV series)">Kung Fu</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Six_Million_Dollar_Man" title="The Six Million Dollar Man">The Six Million Dollar Man</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Wonder_Woman_(TV_series)" title="Wonder Woman (TV series)">Wonder Woman</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Starsky_%26_Hutch" title="Starsky &amp; Hutch">Starsky &amp; Hutch</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Charlie%27s_Angels" title="Charlie's Angels">Charlie's Angels</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bionic_Woman" title="The Bionic Woman">The Bionic Woman</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Fantasy_Island_(1977_TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fantasy Island (1977 TV series)">Fantasy Island (1977 TV series)</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Battlestar_Galactica" title="Battlestar Galactica">Battlestar Galactica</a></i>. Many of these series were greenlit by Silverman, who left ABC in 1978 to become president of NBC's entertainment division. The rousing success of <i>Happy Days</i> also led to a successful spin-off series, <i><a href="/wiki/Laverne_%26_Shirley" title="Laverne &amp; Shirley">Laverne &amp; Shirley</a></i>, which debuted during the 1975–76 season. <i>Charlie's Angels</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Three%27s_Company" title="Three's Company">Three's Company</a></i> (which debuted during the 1976–77 season) were two prime examples of a trend among the major networks during the 1970s known as "<a href="/wiki/Jiggle_TV" class="mw-redirect" title="Jiggle TV">jiggle TV</a>", featuring attractive, often buxom, women in main and guest roles.</p>
<p>In 1977, Henry Plitt, who at the time was associated with Thomas Klutznick, a real estate entrepreneur in Chicago, purchased the southern division of ABC Theatres, ABC Southern.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993231_110-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993231-110">[110]</a></sup> The sale stripped ABC of control over its theaters as a result of changes in the theater operation sector, mainly the fact that the population was migrating to the suburbs and moving away from older cinemas in larger cities<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993231_110-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993231-110">[110]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Plitt_Theatres" title="Plitt Theatres">Plitt Theatres</a> was later purchased by <a href="/wiki/Cineplex_Odeon_Corporation" title="Cineplex Odeon Corporation">Cineplex Odeon Corporation</a> in 1987<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993231_110-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993231-110">[110]</a></sup>).</p>
<p>For its part, the television network produced a few new hits during 1977: January saw the premiere of <i><a href="/wiki/Roots_(1977_miniseries)" title="Roots (1977 miniseries)">Roots</a></i>, a miniseries based on an <a href="/wiki/Alex_Haley" title="Alex Haley">Alex Haley</a> <a href="/wiki/Roots_(novel)" class="mw-redirect" title="Roots (novel)">novel</a> that was published the previous year; in September, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Love_Boat" title="The Love Boat">The Love Boat</a></i>, a comedy-drama anthology series produced by Aaron Spelling which was based around the crew of a cruise ship and featured three stories centered partly on the ship's various passengers; although critically lambasted, the series turned out to be a ratings success and lasted nine seasons. <i>Roots</i> went on to become one of the highest-rated programs in American television history, with unprecedented <a href="/wiki/Nielsen_ratings" title="Nielsen ratings">ratings</a> for its finale.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHyatt2012167_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHyatt2012167-121">[121]</a></sup> The success of <i>Roots</i>, <i>Happy Days</i> and <i>The Love Boat</i> allowed the network to take first place in the ratings for the first time in the <a href="/wiki/1976%E2%80%9377_United_States_network_television_schedule" title="1976–77 United States network television schedule">1976–77 season</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993232_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993232-70">[70]</a></sup> On September 13, 1977, the network debuted <i><a href="/wiki/Soap_(TV_series)" title="Soap (TV series)">Soap</a></i>, a controversial soap opera parody which became known for being the first television series to feature an openly gay main character (played by a then-unknown <a href="/wiki/Billy_Crystal" title="Billy Crystal">Billy Crystal</a>); it last ran on the network on April 20, 1981.</p>
<p>Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/ABC_News" title="ABC News">ABC News</a>, which formed as a newly separate division, sought to become a global leader in television news.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993391_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993391-122">[122]</a></sup> In 1977, Roone Arledge was named president of the new ABC News in addition to being president of ABC Sports.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray19991_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray19991-123">[123]</a></sup> That same year, ABC launched a major expansion of its office facilities in New York City. The company first constructed a new 10-story building on land previously occupied by an abandoned warehouse on the corner of <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Avenue_(Manhattan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbus Avenue (Manhattan)">Columbus Avenue</a> and West 66th Street; the facility that was built in its place is nicknamed "7 Lincoln Square" (although it is actually located at 149 Columbus Avenue). Meanwhile, a former parking lot, located at 30 West 67th Street, was transformed into an impressive 15-story building. Both buildings were completed in June 1979. WABC-TV moved its operations from offices at 77 West 66th Street to 149 Columbus Avenue, freeing up space for the ABC network to house some of its operations.</p>
<p>In June 1978, Arledge created the <a href="/wiki/Newsmagazine" class="mw-redirect" title="Newsmagazine">newsmagazine</a> <i><a href="/wiki/20/20_(U.S._TV_series)" title="20/20 (U.S. TV series)">20/20</a></i>;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993412_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993412-124">[124]</a></sup> after its first episode received harshly negative reviews, the program – which debuted as a summer series, before becoming a year-round program in 1979 – was immediately revamped to feature a mix of in-depth stories and interviews, with <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Downs" title="Hugh Downs">Hugh Downs</a> appointed as its anchor (later paired alongside his former <i>Today</i> colleague <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Walters" title="Barbara Walters">Barbara Walters</a>). In February 1979, ABC sold its recording division to <a href="/wiki/MCA_Inc." title="MCA Inc.">MCA Inc.</a> for $20 million; the label was discontinued by March 5 of that year, and all of its 300 employees were laid off<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronson2003194_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronson2003194-125">[125]</a></sup> (the rights to the works of ABC Records and all of MCA's other labels have since been acquired by <a href="/wiki/Universal_Music_Group" title="Universal Music Group">Universal Music Group</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Merger_with_Capital_Cities.2C_purchase_of_ESPN_and_reprogramming_Friday_nights_.281981.E2.80.931990.29">Merger with Capital Cities, purchase of ESPN and reprogramming Friday nights (1981–1990)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Broadcasting_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Merger with Capital Cities, purchase of ESPN and reprogramming Friday nights (1981–1990)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>ABC dominated the American television landscape during the 1970s and early 1980s (by 1980, the three major networks represented 90% of all prime-time television viewership in the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-bigthree_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bigthree-126">[126]</a></sup>). Several flagship series debuted on the network during this time including <i><a href="/wiki/Dynasty_(TV_series)" title="Dynasty (TV series)">Dynasty</a></i>, an opulent drama from Aaron Spelling that became a hit when it premiered as a midseason series in 1981, five months before Spelling's other ABC hit <i>Charlie's Angels</i> ended its run. The network was also propelled during the early 1980s by the continued successes of <i>Happy Days</i>, <i>Three's Company</i>, <i>Laverne &amp; Shirley</i> and <i>Fantasy Island</i>, and gained new hits in <i><a href="/wiki/Too_Close_for_Comfort_(TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Too Close for Comfort (TV series)">Too Close for Comfort</a></i>, <i>Soap</i> spinoff <i><a href="/wiki/Benson_(TV_series)" title="Benson (TV series)">Benson</a></i> and <i>Happy Days</i> spinoff <i><a href="/wiki/Mork_%26_Mindy" title="Mork &amp; Mindy">Mork &amp; Mindy</a></i>. In 1981, ABC (through its ABC Video Services division) launched the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Repertory_Television_Service" title="Alpha Repertory Television Service">Alpha Repertory Television Service</a> (ARTS), a cable channel operated as a joint venture with the <a href="/wiki/Hearst_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Hearst Corporation">Hearst Corporation</a> offering cultural and arts programming, which aired as a nighttime service over the channel space of <a href="/wiki/Nickelodeon" title="Nickelodeon">Nickelodeon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993429_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993429-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ESPN_identification_card.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/ESPN_identification_card.jpg/220px-ESPN_identification_card.jpg" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/ESPN_identification_card.jpg/330px-ESPN_identification_card.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/ESPN_identification_card.jpg/440px-ESPN_identification_card.jpg 2x" data-file-width="460" data-file-height="276" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ESPN_identification_card.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
ESPN (logo pictured) was acquired by ABC in 1982.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On August 9, 1982, ABC purchased a 10% stake in the Entertainment and Sports Programming Network (ESPN) for $20 million; in exchange for the interest, ESPN gained the U.S. television rights to the <a href="/wiki/British_Open" class="mw-redirect" title="British Open">British Open</a>, which ABC had not been able to broadcast in its entirety.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreeman2002121_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreeman2002121-128">[128]</a></sup> The purchase provided ABC the option of purchasing additional shares of up to 49% under certain conditions,<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_1984_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_1984-129">[129]</a></sup> which included the option to purchase at least 10% of <a href="/wiki/Getty_Oil" title="Getty Oil">Getty Oil</a>'s shares in the channel prior to January 2, 1984.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_1984_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_1984-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1983, ABC sold KXYZ to the <a href="/wiki/Infinity_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="Infinity Broadcasting Corporation">Infinity Broadcasting Corporation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrant1988214_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrant1988214-130">[130]</a></sup> On January 4, 1984, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> reported that ABC, through its subsidiary ABC Video Enterprises, had exercised its option to purchase up to 15% (or between $25 million and $30 million) of Getty Oil's shares in ESPN, which would allow it to expand its shares at a later date.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_1984_129-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_1984-129">[129]</a></sup> In June 1984, ABC's executive committee approved the company's interest acquisition in ESPN, and ABC arranged with Getty Oil to obtain an 80% stake in the channel, while selling the remaining 20% to <a href="/wiki/Nabisco" title="Nabisco">Nabisco</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993439_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993439-131">[131]</a></sup> That year, ABC and Hearst reached an agreement with RCA to merge ARTS and competing arts service, The Entertainment Channel, into a single cable channel called Arts &amp; Entertainment Television (<a href="/wiki/A%26E_(TV_network)" title="A&amp;E (TV network)">A&amp;E</a>); the new channel subsequently leased a separate satellite transponder, ending its sharing agreement with Nickelodeon to become a 24-hour service.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993429_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993429-127">[127]</a></sup> Meanwhile, ABC withdrew from the theme park business for good when it sold the Silver Springs Nature Theme Park.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993424_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993424-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 1984, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Murphy_(broadcasting)" title="Thomas Murphy (broadcasting)">Thomas S. Murphy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chief_executive_officer" title="Chief executive officer">chief executive officer</a> of <a href="/wiki/Capital_Cities_Communications" title="Capital Cities Communications">Capital Cities Communications</a>, contacted Leonard Goldenson about a proposal to merge their respective companies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHagstrom1994.E2.80.931997132_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHagstrom1994.E2.80.931997132-132">[132]</a></sup> On March 16, 1985, ABC's executive committee accepted the merger offer,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993466_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993466-133">[133]</a></sup> which was formally announced on March 18, 1985, with Capital Cities purchasing ABC and its related properties for $3.5 billion and $118 for each of ABC's shares as well as a guarantee of 10% (or $3) for a total of $121 per share.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHagstrom1994.E2.80.931997132_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHagstrom1994.E2.80.931997132-132">[132]</a></sup> To finance the purchase, Capital Cities borrowed $2.1 billion from a consortium of banks, which sold certain assets that Capital Cites could not acquire or retain due to FCC ownership rules for a combined $900 million and sold off several cable television systems, which were sold to <a href="/wiki/Graham_Holdings_Company" title="Graham Holdings Company">The Washington Post Company</a> (forming the present-day <a href="/wiki/Cable_One" title="Cable One">Cable One</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHagstrom1994.E2.80.931997133_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHagstrom1994.E2.80.931997133-134">[134]</a></sup> The remaining $500 million was loaned by <a href="/wiki/Warren_Buffett" title="Warren Buffett">Warren Buffett</a>, who promised that his company <a href="/wiki/Berkshire_Hathaway" title="Berkshire Hathaway">Berkshire Hathaway</a> would purchase $3 million in shares, at $172.50 apiece.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHagstrom1994.E2.80.931997133_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHagstrom1994.E2.80.931997133-134">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993465_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993465-135">[135]</a></sup> Due to an FCC ban on same-market ownership of television and radio stations by a single company (although the deal would have otherwise complied with new ownership rules implemented by the FCC in January 1985, that allowed broadcasters to own a maximum of 12 television stations<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHagstrom1994.E2.80.931997132_132-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHagstrom1994.E2.80.931997132-132">[132]</a></sup>), ABC and Capital Cities respectively decided to sell WXYZ-TV and <a href="/wiki/Tampa,_Florida" title="Tampa, Florida">Tampa</a> independent station <a href="/wiki/WFTS-TV" title="WFTS-TV">WFTS-TV</a> to the <a href="/wiki/E._W._Scripps_Company" title="E. W. Scripps Company">E. W. Scripps Company</a> (although Capital Cities/ABC originally intended to seek a cross-ownership waiver to retain WXYZ and Capital Cities-owned radio stations <a href="/wiki/WJR_(AM)" class="mw-redirect" title="WJR (AM)">WJR</a> and <a href="/wiki/WDVD" title="WDVD">WHYT</a>).</p>
<p>The merger between <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting-Paramount_Theatres" title="American Broadcasting-Paramount Theatres">ABC</a> and Capital Cities received federal approval on September 5, 1985. After the ABC/Capital Cities merger was finalized on January 3, 1986, the combined company – which became known as Capital Cities/ABC, Inc. – added four television stations (WPVI-TV/Philadelphia, <a href="/wiki/KTRK-TV" title="KTRK-TV">KTRK-TV</a>/Houston, <a href="/wiki/KFSN-TV" title="KFSN-TV">KFSN-TV</a>/<a href="/wiki/Fresno,_California" title="Fresno, California">Fresno</a> and <a href="/wiki/WTVD" title="WTVD">WTVD</a>/<a href="/wiki/Raleigh,_North_Carolina" title="Raleigh, North Carolina">Raleigh</a>) and several radio stations to ABC's broadcasting portfolio, and also included <a href="/wiki/Fairchild_Fashion_Media" title="Fairchild Fashion Media">Fairchild Publications</a> and four newspapers (including <i><a href="/wiki/The_Kansas_City_Star" title="The Kansas City Star">The Kansas City Star</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Fort_Worth_Star-Telegram" title="Fort Worth Star-Telegram">Fort Worth Star-Telegram</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> It also initiated several changes in its management:<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Frederick S. Pierce was named president of ABC's broadcasting division; Michael P. Millardi became vice president of ABC Broadcasting, and president of <a href="/wiki/ABC_Owned_Television_Stations" title="ABC Owned Television Stations">ABC Owned Stations</a> and ABC Video Enterprises; John B. Sias was appointed president of the ABC Television Network; <a href="/wiki/Brandon_Stoddard" title="Brandon Stoddard">Brandon Stoddard</a> became president of ABC Entertainment (a position to which he had been appointed in November 1985); and Roone Arledge became president of ABC News and ABC Sports. In February 1986, Thomas S. Murphy, who had been serving as CEO of Capital Cities since 1964, was appointed chairman and CEO emeritus of ABC.<sup id="cite_ref-ChairmanMurphy_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChairmanMurphy-138">[138]</a></sup> Jim Duffy stepped down as ABC Television president for a management position at ABC Communications, a subsidiary that specialized in community service programming, including shows related to literary education.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993391_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993391-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>As far as programming is concerned, four of ABC's marquee shows of the 1970s ended their runs during the mid-1980s: <i>Laverne &amp; Shirley</i> ended its run in 1983, <i>Happy Days</i> and <i>Three's Company</i> ended in 1984 (with the latter producing a <a href="/wiki/Three%27s_a_Crowd" title="Three's a Crowd">short-lived spinoff</a> that year), while <i>The Love Boat</i> ended its run in 1986. After nearly a decade of ratings trouble, NBC had regained the ratings lead among the Big Three networks in 1984 on the success of series such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cosby_Show" title="The Cosby Show">The Cosby Show</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Cheers" title="Cheers">Cheers</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Miami_Vice" title="Miami Vice">Miami Vice</a></i>. To counteract NBC, ABC decided to refocus itself on comedies and family-oriented series beginning in the mid-1980s including <i><a href="/wiki/Who%27s_the_Boss%3F" title="Who's the Boss?">Who's the Boss?</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Mr._Belvedere" title="Mr. Belvedere">Mr. Belvedere</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Growing_Pains" title="Growing Pains">Growing Pains</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Perfect_Strangers_(TV_series)" title="Perfect Strangers (TV series)">Perfect Strangers</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Head_of_the_Class" title="Head of the Class">Head of the Class</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Full_House" title="Full House">Full House</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wonder_Years" title="The Wonder Years">The Wonder Years</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Just_the_Ten_of_Us" title="Just the Ten of Us">Just the Ten of Us</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Roseanne" title="Roseanne">Roseanne</a></i>.</p>
<p>Following the initial success of these series, ABC revamped its Friday night schedule around family-friendly comedies in the late 1980s, culminating in the 1989 debut of the "<a href="/wiki/TGIF_(ABC)" title="TGIF (ABC)">TGIF</a>" block (which promotions referenced stood for "Thank Goodness It's Funny").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPasiuk200512_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPasiuk200512-139">[139]</a></sup> Many of the series featured during the run of the block were produced by <a href="/wiki/Miller-Boyett_Productions" title="Miller-Boyett Productions">Miller-Boyett Productions</a>, a Warner Bros.-based studio that briefly programmed the entire Friday lineup during the <a href="/wiki/1990%E2%80%9391_United_States_network_television_schedule" title="1990–91 United States network television schedule">1990–91 season</a> (with <i><a href="/wiki/Going_Places_(American_TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Going Places (American TV series)">Going Places</a></i> joining <i><a href="/wiki/Family_Matters" title="Family Matters">Family Matters</a></i>, <i>Full House</i> and <i>Perfect Strangers</i> on the "TGIF" schedule) and through its development deal with <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Television" title="Paramount Television">Paramount Television</a> prior to 1986 (as Miller-Milkis, and later, Miller-Milkis-Boyett Productions), had earlier produced <i>Happy Days</i> and its various spinoffs among other series for the network.</p>
<p>In 1988, ABC constructed a new building to serve as the network's headquarters, located near the studios of WABC-TV on West 66th Street. The television network's restructuring program, launched in 1974, helped with the purchases and exchanges of nearly 70 stations during the late 1980s, and aided in increasing its ratings by more than 2 million viewers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993391_122-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldensonWolf1991.E2.80.931993391-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Continued_success_and_acquisition_by_Disney_.281991.E2.80.932000.29">Continued success and acquisition by Disney (1991–2000)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Broadcasting_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Continued success and acquisition by Disney (1991–2000)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1990, Thomas S. Murphy delegated his position as president to <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Burke_(executive)" title="Daniel Burke (executive)">Daniel B. Burke</a> while remaining ABC's chairman and CEO.<sup id="cite_ref-ChairmanMurphy_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChairmanMurphy-138">[138]</a></sup> Capital Cities/ABC reported revenues of $465 million.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHagstrom1994.E2.80.93199769_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHagstrom1994.E2.80.93199769-140">[140]</a></sup> Now at a strong second place, the network entered the 1990s with additional family-friendly hits including <i><a href="/wiki/America%27s_Funniest_Home_Videos" title="America's Funniest Home Videos">America's Funniest Home Videos</a></i> (which has gone on to become the longest-running prime time entertainment program in the network's history), <i><a href="/wiki/Step_by_Step_(TV_series)" title="Step by Step (TV series)">Step by Step</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Hangin%27_with_Mr._Cooper" title="Hangin' with Mr. Cooper">Hangin' with Mr. Cooper</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Boy_Meets_World" title="Boy Meets World">Boy Meets World</a></i> and <i>Perfect Strangers</i> spinoff <i>Family Matters</i>, as well as series such as <i><a href="/wiki/Doogie_Howser,_M.D." title="Doogie Howser, M.D.">Doogie Howser, M.D.</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Life_Goes_On_(TV_series)" title="Life Goes On (TV series)">Life Goes On</a></i>, cult favorite <i><a href="/wiki/Twin_Peaks" title="Twin Peaks">Twin Peaks</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Commish" title="The Commish">The Commish</a></i>. In September 1991, the network premiered <i><a href="/wiki/Home_Improvement_(TV_series)" title="Home Improvement (TV series)">Home Improvement</a></i>, a sitcom starring <a href="/wiki/Stand-up_comedy" title="Stand-up comedy">stand-up comic</a> <a href="/wiki/Tim_Allen" title="Tim Allen">Tim Allen</a> centering on the family and work life of an accident-prone host of a cable-access home improvement show. Lasting eight seasons, its success led ABC to greenlight additional sitcom projects helmed by comedians during the 1990s including <i><a href="/wiki/The_Drew_Carey_Show" title="The Drew Carey Show">The Drew Carey Show</a></i>; <a href="/wiki/Brett_Butler_(actress)" title="Brett Butler (actress)">Brett Butler</a> vehicle <i><a href="/wiki/Grace_Under_Fire_(TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Grace Under Fire (TV series)">Grace Under Fire</a></i>; and <i><a href="/wiki/Ellen_(TV_series)" title="Ellen (TV series)">Ellen</a></i>, which became notable for a <a href="/wiki/The_Puppy_Episode" title="The Puppy Episode">1997 episode</a> which served as the coming out of series star <a href="/wiki/Ellen_DeGeneres" title="Ellen DeGeneres">Ellen DeGeneres</a> (as well as her character in the series) as a <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality" title="Homosexuality">lesbian</a>.</p>
<p>In 1993, the FCC repealed the Financial Interest and Syndication Rules, once again allowing networks to hold interests in television production studios.<sup id="cite_ref-Annual1993_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Annual1993-141">[141]</a></sup> That same year, Capital Cities/ABC purchased the French animation studio <a href="/wiki/DIC_Entertainment" title="DIC Entertainment">DIC Entertainment</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> it also signed an agreement with <a href="/wiki/Time_Warner_Cable" title="Time Warner Cable">Time Warner Cable</a> to carry its owned-and-operated television stations on the provider's systems in ABC O&amp;O markets.<sup id="cite_ref-da000987_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-da000987-143">[143]</a></sup> By that year, ABC had a total viewership share of 23.63% of American households, just below the limit of 25% imposed by the FCC.<sup id="cite_ref-Annual1993_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Annual1993-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>Daniel Burke departed from Capital Cities/ABC in February 1994, with Thomas Murphy taking over as president<sup id="cite_ref-ChairmanMurphy_138-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChairmanMurphy-138">[138]</a></sup> before ceding control to <a href="/wiki/Bob_Iger" title="Bob Iger">Robert Iger</a>. September 1993 saw the debut of <i><a href="/wiki/NYPD_Blue" title="NYPD Blue">NYPD Blue</a></i>, a gritty <a href="/wiki/Police_procedural" title="Police procedural">police procedural</a> from <a href="/wiki/Steven_Bochco" title="Steven Bochco">Steven Bochco</a> (who created <i>Doogie Howser, M.D.</i> and the critically pilloried <i><a href="/wiki/Cop_Rock" title="Cop Rock">Cop Rock</a></i> for ABC earlier in the decade); lasting twelve seasons, the drama became known for its boundary pushing of network television standards (particularly its occasional use of graphic language and rear nudity), which led some affiliates to initially refuse to air the show in its first season.</p>
<p>In order to compete with <a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">CNN</a>, ABC proposed a <a href="/wiki/United_States_cable_news" title="United States cable news">24-hour news channel</a> called ABC Cable News, with plans to launch the network in 1995; however, the plan would ultimately be shelved by company management. ABC would reattempt such a concept in July 2004 with the launch of <a href="/wiki/ABC_News_Now" title="ABC News Now">ABC News Now</a>, a 24-hour news channel distributed for viewing on the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone" title="Mobile phone">mobile phones</a>. On August 29, 1994, ABC purchased <a href="/wiki/Flint,_Michigan" title="Flint, Michigan">Flint, Michigan</a> affiliate <a href="/wiki/WJRT-TV" title="WJRT-TV">WJRT-TV</a> and <a href="/wiki/WTVG" title="WTVG">WTVG</a> in <a href="/wiki/Toledo,_Ohio" title="Toledo, Ohio">Toledo, Ohio</a> (which was previously affiliated with ABC from 1958 to 1970) from <a href="/wiki/Lilly_Broadcasting" title="Lilly Broadcasting">SJL Broadcast Management</a>, with the latter switching to ABC once its contract with NBC expired two months after the purchase was finalized in early 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> Both stations were acquired as a contingency plan in the event that CBS reached an affiliation deal with WXYZ-TV (to replace <a href="/wiki/WJBK" title="WJBK">WJBK</a>, which switched to <a href="/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company" title="Fox Broadcasting Company">Fox</a> as a result of that network's <a href="/wiki/1994_United_States_broadcast_TV_realignment" title="1994 United States broadcast TV realignment">group affiliation agreement</a> with <a href="/wiki/New_World_Pictures#New_World_Communications" title="New World Pictures">New World Communications</a>) in order to allow the network to retain some over-the-air presence in the Detroit market (the E.W. Scripps Company and ABC would reach a group affiliation deal that renewed affiliation agreements with WXYZ and WEWS, and switch four other stations, including two whose Fox affiliations were displaced by the New World deal, with the network).<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 31, 1995, <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Company" title="The Walt Disney Company">The Walt Disney Company</a> announced an agreement to merge with Capital Cities/ABC for $19 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith199824_50-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith199824-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-factbook_1997_4_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook_1997_4-146">[146]</a></sup> Disney shareholders approved the merger at a special conference in New York City on January 4, 1996,<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_1997_4_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook_1997_4-146">[146]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> with the acquisition of Capital Cities/ABC being completed on February 9; following the sale, Disney renamed its new subsidiary ABC Inc.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_1997_4_146-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook_1997_4-146">[146]</a></sup> In addition to the ABC network, the Disney acquisition integrated ABC's ten owned-and-operated television and 21 radio stations; its 80% interest in ESPN, ownership interests in <a href="/wiki/History_(U.S._TV_channel)" title="History (U.S. TV channel)">The History Channel</a>, <a href="/wiki/A%2BE_Networks" class="mw-redirect" title="A+E Networks">A&amp;E Television Networks</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lifetime_(TV_network)" title="Lifetime (TV network)">Lifetime Entertainment</a>; and Capital Cities/ABC's magazine and newspaper properties into the company.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith199824_50-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith199824-50">[50]</a></sup> As FCC ownership rules forbade the company from keeping both it and KABC-TV, Disney sold Los Angeles independent station <a href="/wiki/KCAL-TV" title="KCAL-TV">KCAL-TV</a> to <a href="/wiki/Young_Broadcasting" title="Young Broadcasting">Young Broadcasting</a> for $387 million.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_1997_5_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook_1997_5-148">[148]</a></sup> On April 4, Disney sold the four newspapers that ABC had controlled under Capital Cities to <a href="/wiki/Knight_Ridder" title="Knight Ridder">Knight Ridder</a> for $1.65 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_1997_6_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook_1997_6-149">[149]</a></sup> Following the merger, Thomas S. Murphy left ABC with Robert Iger taking his place as president and CEO.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Around the time of the merger, Disney's television production units had already produced series for the network such as <i>Home Improvement</i> and <i>Boy Meets World</i>, while the deal also allowed ABC access to Disney's children's programming library for its Saturday morning block. In 1998, ABC premiered the <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Sorkin" title="Aaron Sorkin">Aaron Sorkin</a>-created sitcom <i><a href="/wiki/Sports_Night" title="Sports Night">Sports Night</a></i>, centering on the travails of the staff of a <i><a href="/wiki/SportsCenter" title="SportsCenter">SportsCenter</a></i>-style sports news program; despite earning critical praise and multiple <a href="/wiki/Emmy_Award" title="Emmy Award">Emmy Awards</a>, the series was cancelled in 2000 after two seasons.</p>
<p>On May 10, 1999, Disney reorganized its publishing division, the Buena Vista Publishing Group, renaming it as <a href="/wiki/Disney_Publishing_Worldwide" title="Disney Publishing Worldwide">Disney Publishing Worldwide</a>; the rechristened division became a subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/Disney_Consumer_Products" title="Disney Consumer Products">Disney Consumer Products</a> while <a href="/wiki/Hyperion_Books" title="Hyperion Books">Hyperion Books</a> became affiliated with ABC.<sup id="cite_ref-BVPG_reorganization_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BVPG_reorganization-151">[151]</a></sup> On July 8, 1999, Disney consolidated <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Television" title="Walt Disney Television">Walt Disney Television Studio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Disney-ABC_Domestic_Television" class="mw-redirect" title="Disney-ABC Domestic Television">Buena Vista Television Productions</a> and ABC's primetime division into the <a href="/wiki/ABC_Entertainment" title="ABC Entertainment">ABC Entertainment Television Group</a>.</p>
<p>In August 1999, ABC premiered a special series event, <i><a href="/wiki/Who_Wants_to_Be_a_Millionaire_(U.S._game_show)" title="Who Wants to Be a Millionaire (U.S. game show)">Who Wants to Be a Millionaire</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Game_show" title="Game show">game show</a> based on the British program of the same title. Hosted throughout its ABC tenure by <a href="/wiki/Regis_Philbin" title="Regis Philbin">Regis Philbin</a>, the program became a major ratings success throughout its initial summer run, which led ABC to renew <i>Millionaire</i> as a regular series, returning on January 18, 2000. At its peak, the program aired as much as six nights a week.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_2000_7_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook_2000_7-152">[152]</a></sup> Buoyed by <i>Millionaire</i>, during the <a href="/wiki/1999%E2%80%932000_United_States_network_television_schedule" title="1999–2000 United States network television schedule">1999–2000 season</a>, ABC became the first network to move from third to first place in the ratings during a single television season. <i>Millionaire</i> ended its run on the network's primetime lineup after three years in 2002, with Buena Vista Television relaunching the show as a syndicated program (under that incarnation's original host <a href="/wiki/Meredith_Vieira" title="Meredith Vieira">Meredith Vieira</a>) in September of that year.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_century.2C_new_programs.3B_divisional_restructuring_.282001.E2.80.932010.29">New century, new programs; divisional restructuring (2001–2010)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Broadcasting_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: New century, new programs; divisional restructuring (2001–2010)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Times_Square_New_York_City_FLIKR_3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Times_Square_New_York_City_FLIKR_3.jpg/240px-Times_Square_New_York_City_FLIKR_3.jpg" width="240" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Times_Square_New_York_City_FLIKR_3.jpg/360px-Times_Square_New_York_City_FLIKR_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Times_Square_New_York_City_FLIKR_3.jpg/480px-Times_Square_New_York_City_FLIKR_3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Times_Square_New_York_City_FLIKR_3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
ABC's <a href="/wiki/Times_Square_Studios" title="Times Square Studios">Times Square Studios</a> in New York City.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In addition to <i>Who Wants to Be a Millionaire</i>, the network entered the 2000s with hits held over from the previous decade such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Practice" title="The Practice">The Practice</a></i>, <i>NYPD Blue</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_anthology_television_series" title="Walt Disney anthology television series">The Wonderful World of Disney</a></i> and new series such as <i><a href="/wiki/My_Wife_and_Kids" title="My Wife and Kids">My Wife and Kids</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/According_to_Jim" title="According to Jim">According to Jim</a></i>, all of which managed to help ABC stay ahead of the competition in the ratings in spite of the later departure of <i>Millionaire</i>. 2000 saw the end of "TGIF", which was struggling to find new hits (with <i>Boy Meets World</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Sabrina,_the_Teenage_Witch_(TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sabrina, the Teenage Witch (TV series)">Sabrina, the Teenage Witch</a></i>, the latter of which moved to <a href="/wiki/The_WB" title="The WB">The WB</a> in October 2006, beginning to wane as well by this point) following the loss of <i>Family Matters</i> and <i>Step by Step</i> to CBS as part of its own <a href="/wiki/CBS_Block_Party" title="CBS Block Party">failed attempt at a family-oriented Friday comedy block</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1997%E2%80%9398_United_States_network_television_schedule" title="1997–98 United States network television schedule">1997–98 season</a>. Outside of <i>20/20</i>, Friday nights remained a weak spot for ABC for the next 11 years.</p>
<p>On April 30, 2000, as a result of a <a href="/wiki/Carriage_dispute" title="Carriage dispute">carriage dispute</a> with ABC, Time Warner Cable removed ABC owned-and-operated stations from the cable provider's systems in four markets (WABC-TV in New York City, KABC-TV in Los Angeles, KTRK in Houston and WTVD in Raleigh-Durham). The network had earlier reached an eleventh-hour deal to renew its carriage agreement with the provider on December 31, 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-da000987_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-da000987-143">[143]</a></sup> ABC filed an emergency petition to the Federal Communications Commission on May 1 to force TWC to restore the affected stations; the FCC ruled in favor of ABC, ordering Time Warner Cable to restore the stations, doing so on the afternoon of May 2.<sup id="cite_ref-da000987_143-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-da000987-143">[143]</a></sup> ABC ended the <a href="/wiki/2000%E2%80%9301_United_States_network_television_schedule" title="2000–01 United States network television schedule">2000–01</a> season as the most-watched network, ahead of NBC.</p>
<p>Networks affiliates approved a two-year affiliate agreement in 2002. In September 2006, Disney Chairman/CEO <a href="/wiki/Michael_Eisner" title="Michael Eisner">Michael Eisner</a> outlined a proposed realignment of the ABC broadcast network day parts with the similar unit in its cable channels: ABC Sunday mornings with <a href="/wiki/Disney_Channel" title="Disney Channel">Disney Channels</a> (<a href="/wiki/Toon_Disney" title="Toon Disney">Toon</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Playhouse_Disney" title="Playhouse Disney">Playhouse</a>), ABC daytime with <a href="/wiki/Soapnet" title="Soapnet">Soapnet</a> and ABC prime time with ABC Family.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> 2002 saw the debut of the network's first hit reality series, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bachelor_(U.S._TV_series)" title="The Bachelor (U.S. TV series)">The Bachelor</a></i> (the elimination-style dating show's success led to a spinoff, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bachelorette" title="The Bachelorette">The Bachelorette</a></i>, which premiered the following year, as well as two <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_Pad" title="Bachelor Pad">additional</a> <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_in_Paradise_(TV_series)" title="Bachelor in Paradise (TV series)">spinoffs</a> that later debuted in the early 2010s).</p>
<p>In 2004, ABC's average viewership declined by ten ratings points, landing the network in fourth place, behind NBC, CBS and Fox (by the following year, the combined season-ending average audience share of ABC, NBC and CBS represented only 32% of U.S. households<sup id="cite_ref-bigthree_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bigthree-126">[126]</a></sup>). However, during the <a href="/wiki/2004%E2%80%9305_United_States_network_television_schedule" title="2004–05 United States network television schedule">2004–05 season</a>, the network experienced unexpected success with new series such as <i><a href="/wiki/Desperate_Housewives" title="Desperate Housewives">Desperate Housewives</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Lost_(TV_series)" title="Lost (TV series)">Lost</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Grey%27s_Anatomy" title="Grey's Anatomy">Grey's Anatomy</a></i> as well as reality series <i><a href="/wiki/Dancing_with_the_Stars" title="Dancing with the Stars">Dancing with the Stars</a></i>, which helped ABC rise to second place, jumping ahead of CBS, but behind a surging Fox. On April 21, 2004, Disney announced a restructuring of its Disney Media Networks division with Marvin Jacobs being named president of ABC parent <a href="/wiki/Disney%E2%80%93ABC_Television_Group" title="Disney–ABC Television Group">Disney–ABC Television Group</a>, and ESPN president <a href="/wiki/George_Bodenheimer" title="George Bodenheimer">George Bodenheimer</a> becoming co-CEO of the division with Jacobs, as well as president of ABC Sports.<sup id="cite_ref-nominations_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nominations-154">[154]</a></sup> On December 7, 2005, ABC Sports and ESPN signed an eight-year broadcast rights agreement with <a href="/wiki/NASCAR" title="NASCAR">NASCAR</a>, allowing ABC and ESPN to broadcast 17 <a href="/wiki/NASCAR_Cup_Series" class="mw-redirect" title="NASCAR Cup Series">NASCAR Cup Series</a> races each season (comprising just over half of the 36 races held annually) effective with the 2006 season.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_2005_20_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook_2005_20-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Separation_of_the_radio_network">Separation of the radio network</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Broadcasting_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Separation of the radio network">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Between May and September 2005, rumors circulated that Disney–ABC was considering a sale of ABC Radio, with <a href="/wiki/Clear_Channel_Communications" class="mw-redirect" title="Clear Channel Communications">Clear Channel Communications</a> and <a href="/wiki/Westwood_One_(1976%E2%80%932011)" title="Westwood One (1976–2011)">Westwood One</a> (which had earlier purchased NBC's radio division, as well as the distribution rights to CBS's, and the Mutual Broadcasting System during the 1990s) as potential buyers. On October 19, 2005, ABC announced the restructuring of the group into six divisions: Entertainment Communications, Communications Resources, Kids Communications, News Communications, Corporate Communications, and International Communications.</p>
<p>On February 6, 2007, The Walt Disney Company announced an agreement with <a href="/wiki/Citadel_Broadcasting" title="Citadel Broadcasting">Citadel Broadcasting</a> to merge the ABC Radio Network with Citadel. The new entity, <a href="/wiki/Citadel_Communications" title="Citadel Communications">Citadel Communications</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_2006_15_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook_2006_15-156">[156]</a></sup> was majority owned (52%) by Disney, in conjunction with <a href="/wiki/Forstmann_Little" class="mw-redirect" title="Forstmann Little">Forstmann Little</a> (32%) and former shareholders of Citadel Broadcasting (16%). Citadel eventually merged with <a href="/wiki/Cumulus_Media" title="Cumulus Media">Cumulus Media</a> in September 2011.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Entertainment_reorganization_and_struggles_with_new_shows_.282007.E2.80.932009.29">Entertainment reorganization and struggles with new shows (2007–2009)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Broadcasting_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Entertainment reorganization and struggles with new shows (2007–2009)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In February 2007, Disney announced that it would rename its Touchstone Television production unit as the ABC Television Studio (simplified to <a href="/wiki/ABC_Studios" title="ABC Studios">ABC Studios</a> by that summer), as part of a corporate move to eliminate secondary production brands such as <a href="/wiki/Buena_Vista_(brand)" title="Buena Vista (brand)">Buena Vista</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WDC-ABC_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WDC-ABC-157">[157]</a></sup> In May 2007, ABC unveiled a new image campaign, revolving around the slogan "Start Here", which highlighted the multi-platform availability of ABC's program content.<sup id="cite_ref-abcnews-starthere_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abcnews-starthere-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%932008_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike" class="mw-redirect" title="2007–2008 Writers Guild of America strike">Writers Guild of America strike</a> that halted production of network programs for much of the <a href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%9308_United_States_network_television_schedule" title="2007–08 United States network television schedule">2007–08 season</a> affected the network in 2007–08 and <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%9309_United_States_network_television_schedule" title="2008–09 United States network television schedule">2008–09</a>, as various ABC shows that premiered in 2007, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Dirty_Sexy_Money" title="Dirty Sexy Money">Dirty Sexy Money</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Pushing_Daisies" title="Pushing Daisies">Pushing Daisies</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Eli_Stone" title="Eli Stone">Eli Stone</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Samantha_Who%3F" title="Samantha Who?">Samantha Who?</a></i>, did not live to see a third season; other series such as <i><a href="/wiki/Boston_Legal" title="Boston Legal">Boston Legal</a></i> and the U.S. version of <i><a href="/wiki/Life_on_Mars_(U.S._TV_series)" title="Life on Mars (U.S. TV series)">Life on Mars</a></i> suffered from low viewership, despite the former, a spin off of <i>The Practice</i>, being a once-highlighted breakout series when it debuted in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> One of the network's strike-replacement programs during that time was the game show <i><a href="/wiki/Duel_(U.S._game_show)" title="Duel (U.S. game show)">Duel</a></i>, which premiered in December 2007. The program would become a minor success for the network during its initial six-episode run, which led ABC to renew <i>Duel</i> as a regular series starting in April 2008. However, <i>Duel</i> suffered from low viewership during its run as a regular series, and ABC canceled the program after sixteen episodes. On August 15, 2008, Disney denied rumors started by Caris &amp; Co. that it would be selling the ten ABC owned-and-operated stations.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>In early 2009, Disney–ABC Television Group merged ABC Entertainment and ABC Studios into a new division, ABC Entertainment Group, which would be responsible for both its production and broadcasting operations.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> During this reorganization, the group announced that it would lay off 5% of its workforce.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> On April 2, 2009, Citadel Communications announced that it would rebrand ABC Radio as Citadel Media;<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> however, ABC News continued to provide news content for Citadel. On December 22, Disney–ABC Television Group announced a partnership with <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple Inc.</a> to make individual episodes of ABC and <a href="/wiki/Disney_Channel" title="Disney Channel">Disney Channel</a> programs available for purchase on <a href="/wiki/ITunes" title="ITunes">iTunes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_state">Current state</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Broadcasting_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Current state">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In March 2010, reports suggested that The Walt Disney Company was considering spinning off ABC into an independent company because "it [did not] add a lot of value to Disney's other divisions".<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> The company entered advanced negotiations with two <a href="/wiki/Private_equity_firm" title="Private equity firm">private equity firms</a> to sell ABC; however, the planned sale was cancelled as a result of an <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">FBI</a> investigation into allegations of attempted insider trading by an ex-employee which they later denied.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<p>The network began running into some trouble in the ratings by 2010. That year, the sixth and final season of <i>Lost</i> became the drama's lowest-rated season since its debut in 2004. Ratings for the once-instant hit <i><a href="/wiki/Ugly_Betty" title="Ugly Betty">Ugly Betty</a></i> collapsed dramatically after it was moved to <a href="/wiki/Friday_night_death_slot" title="Friday night death slot">Fridays</a> at the start of its fourth season in the fall of 2009; an attempt to boost ratings by moving the dramedy to Wednesdays failed, with its ultimate cancellation by the network eliciting negative reaction from the public, and particularly the show's fanbase.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> With the network's two former hit shows now out of the picture, the network's remaining top veteran shows <i>Desperate Housewives</i> and <i>Grey's Anatomy</i>, and another hit drama <i><a href="/wiki/Brothers_%26_Sisters_(2006_TV_series)" title="Brothers &amp; Sisters (2006 TV series)">Brothers &amp; Sisters</a></i>, all ended the <a href="/wiki/2009%E2%80%9310_United_States_network_television_schedule" title="2009–10 United States network television schedule">2009–10 season</a> recorded their lowest ratings ever.</p>
<p>Among the few bright spots during this season were the midseason crime dramedy <i><a href="/wiki/Castle_(TV_series)" title="Castle (TV series)">Castle</a></i> as well as the success of two family sitcoms that anchored the network's revamped Wednesday comedy lineup, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Middle_(TV_series)" title="The Middle (TV series)">The Middle</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Modern_Family" title="Modern Family">Modern Family</a></i>, the latter of which was both a critical and commercial success. <i><a href="/wiki/Shark_Tank_(TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Shark Tank (TV series)">Shark Tank</a></i> (based on the <i><a href="/wiki/Dragon%27s_Den" class="mw-redirect" title="Dragon's Den">Dragon's Den</a></i> reality format) also became a midseason sleeper hit on Sundays in the spring of 2010; the following season, it became the <a href="/wiki/Tent-pole_(entertainment)" title="Tent-pole (entertainment)">tentpole</a> of the network's Friday night schedule, gradually helping make ABC a strong competitor (after being paired with <i>20/20</i> and beginning with the <a href="/wiki/2012%E2%80%9313_United_States_network_television_schedule" title="2012–13 United States network television schedule">2012–13 season</a>, the Tim Allen sitcom <i><a href="/wiki/Last_Man_Standing_(U.S._TV_series)" title="Last Man Standing (U.S. TV series)">Last Man Standing</a></i>) against CBS' long-dominant drama/reality lineup on that night for the first time since the "TGIF" lineup ended in 2000.</p>
<p>The network's troubles with sustaining existing series and gaining new hits spilled over into its <a href="/wiki/2010%E2%80%9311_United_States_network_television_schedule" title="2010–11 United States network television schedule">2010–11 schedule</a>: ABC's dramas during that season continued to fail, with the midseason forensic investigation drama <i><a href="/wiki/Body_of_Proof" title="Body of Proof">Body of Proof</a></i> being the only one that was renewed for a second season.<sup id="cite_ref-ABC_Renewals_Spring_2011_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ABC_Renewals_Spring_2011-172">[172]</a></sup> The network also struggled to establish new comedies to support the previous year's debuts, with only late-season premiere <i><a href="/wiki/Happy_Endings_(TV_series)" title="Happy Endings (TV series)">Happy Endings</a></i> earning a second season.<sup id="cite_ref-ABC_Renewals_Spring_2011_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ABC_Renewals_Spring_2011-172">[172]</a></sup> Meanwhile, the new lows hit by <i>Brothers &amp; Sisters</i> led to its cancellation, and the previous year's only drama renewal, <i><a href="/wiki/V_(2009_TV_series)" title="V (2009 TV series)">V</a></i>, also failed to earn another season after a low-rated midseason run.<sup id="cite_ref-ABC_Cancellations_Spring_2011_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ABC_Cancellations_Spring_2011-173">[173]</a></sup> Despite this and another noticeable ratings decline, ABC would manage to outrate NBC for third place by a larger margin than the previous year.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p>With relatively little buzz surrounding its 2010–11 pilots, compounded by a <a href="/wiki/Sexual_harassment" title="Sexual harassment">sexual harassment</a> lawsuit against him, Stephen McPherson resigned as ABC Entertainment Group president on July 27, 2010. <a href="/wiki/Paul_Lee_(television_executive)" title="Paul Lee (television executive)">Paul Lee</a> (who previously served as the president of sister cable channel ABC Family) was announced as his replacement that same day.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p>On April 14, 2011, ABC canceled the long-running soap operas <i><a href="/wiki/All_My_Children" title="All My Children">All My Children</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/One_Life_to_Live" title="One Life to Live">One Life to Live</a></i> after 41 and 43 years on the air, respectively<sup id="cite_ref-LA_Times_cancel_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LA_Times_cancel-177">[177]</a></sup> (following backlash from fans, ABC sold the rights to both shows to <a href="/wiki/Prospect_Park_(production_company)" title="Prospect Park (production company)">Prospect Park</a>, which eventually revived the soaps on Hulu for one additional season in 2013 and with both companies suing one another for allegations of interference with the process of reviving the shows, failure to pay licensing fees and issues over ABC's use of certain characters from <i>One Life to Live</i> on <i>General Hospital</i> during the transition<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup>). The talk/lifestyle show that replaced <i>One Life to Live</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Revolution_(TV_series)" title="The Revolution (TV series)">The Revolution</a></i>, failed to generate satisfactory ratings and was in turn canceled after only seven months. The <a href="/wiki/2011%E2%80%9312_United_States_network_television_schedule" title="2011–12 United States network television schedule">2011–12 season</a> saw ABC drop to fourth place in the 18–49 demographic despite renewing a handful of new shows (including freshmen dramas <i><a href="/wiki/Scandal_(TV_series)" title="Scandal (TV series)">Scandal</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Revenge_(TV_series)" title="Revenge (TV series)">Revenge</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Once_Upon_a_Time_(TV_series)" title="Once Upon a Time (TV series)">Once Upon a Time</a></i>) for second seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup></p>
